'''China'''&lt;!--Please do not add official regional/minority languages here; use the langbox template directly above, included specifically for that purpose--&gt;, officially the '''People's Republic of China''' ('''PRC'''), located in Asia, is the world's [[List of countries and dependencies by population|most populous state]], with a population of over [[Demographics of China|1.381&amp;nbsp;billion]].&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web|url=http://www.worldometers.info/chinapopulation|title=China Population|date=15 March 2016|website=www.worldometers.info|access-date=15 March 2016}}&lt;/ref&gt; The state is governed by the [[Communist Party of China]] based in the [[Historical capitals of China|capital]] of [[Beijing]].{{sfnb|Shambaugh|2008| p= [https://books.google.com/books?id=aMpj-MboMR4C&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq=China+one-party+state&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=0ahUKEwiR44ThieTKAhUHYiYKHUE8C0A4PBDoAQg-MAY#v=onepage&amp;q=one-party&amp;f=false 3 etc.]}} It exercises [[jurisdiction]] over 22 [[Provinces of China|provinces]]&lt;!-- these are ONLY the provinces the PRC has jurisdictional control over. It is already mentioned as disputed in the &quot;Administrative divisions&quot; section below. --&gt;, five [[Autonomous regions of China|autonomous regions]], four [[Direct-controlled municipalities of China|direct-controlled municipalities]] (Beijing, [[Tianjin]], [[Shanghai]], and [[Chongqing]]), two mostly self-governing [[special administrative region]]s ([[Hong Kong]] and [[Macau]]), and claims sovereignty over [[Taiwan]]. China is a [[great power]] and a major [[regional power]] within Asia, and has been characterized as a [[Potential superpowers|potential superpower]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web |url=http://www.carnegieendowment.org/events/index.cfm?fa=eventDetail&amp;id=851&amp;prog=zch |title=From Rural Transformation to Global Integration: The Environmental and Social Impacts of China's Rise to Superpower |last=Muldavin |first=Joshua |date=9 February 2006 |publisher=Carnegie Endowment for International Peace |accessdate=17 January 2010 }}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;ChinaFuture&quot;&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/magazine-19995218|title=A Point Of View: What kind of superpower could China be?|publisher=BBC|date=19 October 2012|accessdate=21 October 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;

Covering approximately 9.6&amp;nbsp;million square kilometers, China is the world's [[List of countries and outlying territories by land area|second largest state by land area]],&lt;ref name=&quot;listofcountriesoftheworld.com&quot;&gt;{{cite web |url=http://www.listofcountriesoftheworld.com/area-land.html |title=Countries of the world ordered by land area|publisher=Listofcountriesoftheworld.com |accessdate=27 April 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; and either the [[List of countries and outlying territories by total area|third or fourth-largest by total area]], depending on the method of measurement.{{efn|The total area ranking relative to the United States depends on the measurement of the total areas of China and the United States. See [[List of countries and outlying territories by area]] for more information.}} China's landscape is vast and diverse, ranging from [[Mongolian-Manchurian grassland|forest steppes]] and the [[Gobi Desert|Gobi]] and [[Taklamakan Desert|Taklamakan]] deserts in the arid north to [[subtropical]] forests in the wetter south. The [[Himalaya]], [[Karakoram]], [[Pamir Mountains|Pamir]] and [[Tian Shan]] mountain ranges separate China from [[South Asia|south]] and [[Central Asia|central]] Asia. The [[Yangtze River|Yangtze]] and [[Yellow River|Yellow]] rivers, the third and sixth longest in the world, respectively, run from the [[Tibetan Plateau]] to the densely populated eastern seaboard. China's coastline along the Pacific Ocean is {{convert|14500|km|mi|sp=us}} long, and is bounded by the [[Bohai Sea|Bohai]], [[Yellow Sea|Yellow]], [[East China Sea|East China]], and [[South China Sea|South China]] seas.

China is a [[cradle of civilization]], with its known history beginning with an ancient civilization â€“ one of the world's earliest â€“ that flourished in the fertile basin of the [[Yellow River]] in the [[North China Plain]]. For millennia, China's political system was based on hereditary monarchies known as [[Dynasties in Chinese history|dynasties]]. Since 221&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}}, when the [[Qin Dynasty]] first conquered several states to form a Chinese empire, the state has expanded, fractured and reformed numerous times. The [[Republic of China (1912â€“1949)|Republic of China]] (ROC) replaced the [[Qing Dynasty|last dynasty]] in 1912, and ruled the [[mainland China|Chinese mainland]] until 1949, when it was defeated by the Communist Party of China in the [[Chinese Civil War]]. The Communist Party established the People's Republic of China in Beijing on 1 October 1949, while the ROC government relocated to Taiwan with its present capital in [[Taipei]]. Both the ROC and PRC continue to claim to be the legitimate government of all China.

China had the largest economy in the world for most of the past two thousand years, during which it has seen cycles of [[prosperity]] and decline.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.eric.ed.gov/ERICWebPortal/custom/portlets/recordDetails/detailmini.jsp?_nfpb=true&amp;_&amp;ERICExtSearch_SearchValue_0=ED460052&amp;ERICExtSearch_SearchType_0=no&amp;accno=ED460052|last=Dahlman|first= Carl J|last2= Aubert|first2=Jean-Eric |title=China and the Knowledge Economy: Seizing the 21st Century. WBI Development Studies. World Bank Publications. |publisher=Institute of Education Sciences |accessdate=26 July 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;http://browse.oecdbookshop.org/oecd/pdfs/product/4107091e.pdf Angus Maddison. Chinese Economic
Performance in the Long Run. Development Centre Studies. Accessed 2007. p.29&lt;/ref&gt; Since the introduction of [[Chinese economic reform|economic reforms in 1978]], China has become one of the world's [[List of countries by real GDP growth rate|fastest-growing]] major economies. {{As of|2014}}, it is the world's second-largest economy by [[List of countries by GDP (nominal)|nominal GDP]] and largest by [[List of countries by GDP (PPP)|purchasing power parity]] (PPP). China is also the world's largest exporter and second-largest importer of goods.&lt;ref name=&quot;ChinaBiggestTrader&quot;&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.telegraph.co.uk/finance/economics/9860518/China-trade-now-bigger-than-US.html|title=China trade now bigger than US|work=Daily Telegraph|date=10 February 2013|accessdate=15 February 2013|location=London|first=Garry|last=White}}&lt;/ref&gt; China is a recognized [[List of states with nuclear weapons|nuclear weapons state]] and has the world's [[List of countries by number of active troops|largest standing army]] and [[List of countries by military expenditures|second-largest defense budget]].&lt;ref name=&quot;ChineseNukes&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=SIPRI2014/&gt; The PRC is a member of the [[United Nations]], as it replaced the ROC as a [[Permanent members of the United Nations Security Council|permanent member]] of the [[United Nations Security Council|U.N. Security Council]] in 1971. China is also a member of numerous formal and informal multilateral organizations, including the [[World Trade Organization|WTO]], [[Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation|APEC]], [[BRICS]], the [[Shanghai Cooperation Organization]] (SCO), the [[BCIM]] and the [[G-20 major economies|G-20]].

{{Chinese text|compact=yes|image=}}
The English name &quot;China&quot; is first attested in [[Richard Eden]]'s 1555 translation{{efn|&quot;...Next vnto this, is found the great ''China'', whose kyng is thought to bee the greatest [[prince]] in the worlde, and is named Santoa Raia...&quot;&lt;ref&gt;[[Richard Eden|Eden, Richard]] (1555), ''Decades of the New World'', [http://quod.lib.umich.edu/e/eebo/a20032.0001.001/511?page=root;size=125;vid=4616;view=text p. 230].&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book | title=Western Views of China and the Far East, Volume 1 | publisher=Asian Research Service | year=1984 | page=34 |first=Henry Allen |last=Myers}}&lt;/ref&gt;}} of the 1516 journal of the [[Portuguese empire|Portuguese]] [[Portuguese exploration|explorer]] [[Duarte Barbosa]].{{efn|&quot;...The Very Great Kingdom of China...&quot;&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |url=https://books.google.com.vn/books?id=edzW9fuOF-cC |p=[https://books.google.com.vn/books?id=edzW9fuOF-cC&amp;pg=PA211 211] |title=The Book of Duarte Barbosa, ''Vol. II'' |last=Barbosa |first=Duarte |display-authors=0 |editor-last=Dames |editor-first=Mansel Longworth |location=London |date=1918 }}.&lt;/ref&gt; ({{lang-pt|...O Grande Reino da China...}}).&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |first=Duarte |last=Barbosa |title=Livro em que dÃ¡ RelaÃ§Ã£o do que Viu e Ouviu no Oriente |editor=Augusto Reis Machado |display-editors=0 |location=Lisbon |date=1946 |url=http://purl.pt/435 }}. {{pt icon}}&lt;/ref&gt;}}&lt;ref&gt;&quot;China&quot; in the ''Oxford English Dictionary'' (1989). ISBN 0-19-957315-8.&lt;/ref&gt; The demonym and adjectival form &quot;Chinese&quot; developed later on the model of Portuguese ''{{lang|pt|chinÃªs}}'' and French ''{{lang|fr|chinois}}''.&lt;ref&gt;&quot;-ese, ''suffix''&quot;, and &quot;Chinese, ''adj.'' and ''n.''&quot;, in the ''Oxford English Dictionary'', Oxford: Oxford University Press.&lt;/ref&gt;{{efn|Eden used the now obsolete form ''Chinish'': &quot;...whence the Chinishe nation haue theyr prouision for shppyng...&quot;&lt;ref&gt;[[Richard Eden|Eden, Richard]] in R. Willes (1577). ''The History of Trauayle in the West and East Indies'', p. 260&lt;/ref&gt;}} Portuguese ''China'' is thought to derive from [[Persian language|Persian]] ''ChÄ«n'' ({{lang|fa|{{linktext|Ú†ÛŒÙ†}}}}){{efn|Originally also used for [[East Turkestan]].}} and, ultimately, from [[Sanskrit]] ''[[Chinas|CÄ«na]]'' ({{lang|sa|{{linktext|à¤šà¥€à¤¨}}}}).&lt;ref name=&quot;AmHer&quot;&gt;&quot;[http://dictionary.reference.com/browse/China?qsrc=2888 China]&quot;. ''The American Heritage Dictionary of the English Language'' (2000). Boston and New York: Houghton-Mifflin.&lt;/ref&gt; ''CÄ«na'' was first used in early [[Hindu]] scripture, including the ''[[MahÄ?bhÄ?rata]]'' (5th century&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}}) and the ''[[Manusmá¹›ti|Laws of Manu]]'' (2nd century&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}}).&lt;ref name=wade&gt;Wade, Geoff. &quot;[http://www.sino-platonic.org/complete/spp188_yelang_china.pdf The Polity of Yelang and the Origin of the Name 'China']&quot;. ''[[Sino-Platonic Papers]]'', No. 188, May 2009, p. 20.&lt;/ref&gt; The traditional theory, proposed in the 17th century by [[Martino Martini]]&lt;ref name=&quot;Martini&quot;&gt;Martino, Martin, ''Novus Atlas Sinensis'', Vienna 1655, Preface, p. 2.&lt;/ref&gt; and supported by many later scholars, is that the word &quot;China&quot; and its earlier related forms are ultimately derived from the state of [[Qin (state)|Qin]] ({{lang|zh-hans|{{linktext|ç§¦}}}}, [[Old Chinese]]:&amp;nbsp;*''Dzin''),&lt;ref name=bs/&gt; the westernmost of the [[list of ancient Chinese states|Chinese states]] during the [[Zhou dynasty]] which unified China to form the [[Qin dynasty]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|title=The Clash of Empires: the invention of China in modern world making|last=Liu|first=Lydia He|publisher=[[Harvard University Press]]|year=2009|pages=77â€“78|isbn=9780674040298|quote=[[Patrick Olivelle|Olivelle]]'s evidence affirms that ''cÄ«na'' is related to the Qin dynasty but leaves the precise nature of that linkage open to speculation.}}&lt;/ref&gt; There are, however, other suggestions for the derivation of &quot;China&quot;.&lt;ref name=wade /&gt;

The official name of the modern state is the People's Republic of China {{nowrap|({{zh| s={{linktext|ä¸­å?Žäººæ°‘å…±å’Œå›½}}| hp=ZhÅ?nghuÃ¡ RÃ©nmÃ­n GÃ²nghÃ©guÃ³}}).}} The shorter form for &quot;China&quot; is ''{{lang|zh-latn-pinyin|ZhÅ?ngguÃ³}}'' {{nowrap|({{lang|zh|{{linktext|ä¸­å›½}}}}),}} from ''{{lang|zh-latn-pinyin|zhÅ?ng}}'' (&quot;central&quot; or &quot;middle&quot;) and ''{{lang|zh-latn-pinyin|guÃ³}}'' (&quot;state, nation-state&quot;),&lt;ref name=zg/&gt;{{efn|Although this is the present meaning of ''{{lang|zh-latn-pinyin|guÃ³}}'', in [[Old Chinese]] (when its pronunciation was something like {{nowrap|/*qÊ·Ë¤É™k/}})&lt;ref name=bs&gt;[[Reconstructions of Old Chinese|Baxter-Sagart]].&lt;/ref&gt; it meant the walled city of the Chinese and the areas they could control from them.&lt;ref name=wilx/&gt;}} a term which developed under the [[Western Zhou|Zhou]] [[list of Chinese dynasties|Dynasty]] in reference to its [[demesne|royal demesne]].{{efn|Its use is attested from the 6th-century [[Classic of History]], which states &quot;[[Tian (god)|Huangtian]] bestowed the lands and the peoples of the central state to the ancestors&quot; ({{lang|zh|çš‡å¤©æ—¢ä»˜ä¸­åœ‹æ°‘è¶ŠåŽ¥ç–†åœŸäºŽå…ˆçŽ‹}}).&lt;ref&gt;{{lang|zh|[[:s:zh:å°šæ›¸|ã€Šå°šæ›¸ã€‹]], [[:s:zh:å°šæ›¸/æ¢“æ??|æ¢“æ??]].}} {{zh icon}}&lt;/ref&gt;}} It was then applied to the area around [[Luoyi]] (present-day Luoyang) during the [[Eastern Zhou]] and then to China's [[Central Plain (China)|Central Plain]] before being used as an occasional synonym for the state under the [[Qing dynasty|Qing]].&lt;ref name=wilx&gt;{{citation |last=Wilkinson |first=Endymion |title=Chinese History: A Manual |url=https://books.google.com.hk/books?id=ERnrQq0bsPYC&amp;printsec=frontcover |date=2000 |location=[[Cambridge, Mass.|Cambridge]] |publisher=Harvard University Asia Center |series=Harvard-Yenching Institute Monograph No. 52 |p=[https://books.google.com.hk/books?id=ERnrQq0bsPYC&amp;pg=PA132 132] }}.&lt;/ref&gt; It was often used as a cultural concept to distinguish the [[Huaxia]] tribes from [[Hua-Yi distinction|perceived &quot;barbarians&quot;]]&lt;ref name=wilx/&gt; and was the source of the English name {{nowrap|&quot;Middle Kingdom&quot;.}}&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|last=Tang|first=Xiaoyang|title=Greater China in an Era of Globalization|year=2010|publisher=Rowman &amp; Littlefield Publishers|location=Lanham, MD|isbn=978-0-7391-3534-1|pages=52â€“53|editor=Guo, Sujian |editor2=Guo, Baogang}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|last=Challen|first=Paul|title=Life in ancient China|year=2005|publisher=Crabtree Publishing|location=New York|isbn=978-0-7787-2037-9|page=6}}&lt;/ref&gt; A more literary or inclusive name, alluding to the &quot;land of [[Chinese civilization]]&quot;, is ''{{lang|zh-latn-pinyin|ZhÅ?nghuÃ¡}}'' {{nowrap|({{lang|zh|{{linktext|ä¸­å?Ž}}}}).}}&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |series=''Routledge Research on Taiwan'' |publisher=Routledge |location=Abingdon |url=https://books.google.com.hk/books?id=wjaLBQAAQBAJ |p=[https://books.google.com.hk/books?id=wjaLBQAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PT220 220] |title=Language, Politics, and Identity in Taiwan: Naming China |author=Hui-Ching Chang |author2=Richard Holt |display-authors=1 }}.&lt;/ref&gt; It developed during the [[Cao Wei|Wei]] and [[Sima Jin|Jin]] as a contraction of &quot;the central state of the [[Huaxia]]&quot;.&lt;ref name=wilx/&gt;

==History==
{{Main article|History of China|Timeline of Chinese history}}

===Prehistory===
{{main article|Chinese prehistory}}
Archaeological evidence suggests that early [[Hominidae|hominids]] inhabited China between 2.24 million and 250,000 years ago.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.archaeology.org/0001/newsbriefs/china.html &quot;Early Homo erectus Tools in China&quot;]. Archaeological Institute of America. 2000. Retrieved 30 November 2012.&lt;/ref&gt; The hominid fossils of [[Peking Man]], a ''[[Homo erectus]]'' who [[Control of fire by early humans|used fire]],&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.unesco.org/ext/field/beijing/whc/pkm-site.htm|title=The Peking Man World Heritage Site at Zhoukoudian|publisher=UNESCO|accessdate=6 March 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; were discovered in a cave at [[Zhoukoudian]] near [[Beijing]]; they have been dated to between 680,000 and 780,000 [[Before Present|years ago]].&lt;ref name=&quot;autogenerated198&quot;&gt;{{Cite journal| doi = 10.1038/nature07741|date=Mar 2009|author1=Shen, G |author2=Gao, X |author3=Gao, B |author4=Granger, De | title = Age of Zhoukoudian Homo erectus determined with (26)Al/(10)Be burial dating| volume = 458| issue = 7235| pages = 198â€“200| issn = 0028-0836| pmid = 19279636| journal = Nature|bibcode = 2009Natur.458..198S }}&lt;/ref&gt; The fossilized teeth of ''Homo sapiens'' (dated to 125,000â€“80,000 [[Before Present|years ago]]) have been discovered in [[Fuyan Cave]] in [[Dao County]], [[Hunan]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.bbc.com/news/science-environment-34531861|title=Fossil teeth place humans in Asia '20,000 years early'|publisher=[[BBC News]]|accessdate=14 October 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; Chinese [[proto-writing]] existed in [[Jiahu]] around 7000&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}},&lt;ref name= &quot;earliest writing&quot;&gt;{{cite news |title='Earliest writing' found in China |first=Paul |last=Rincon |date=17 April 2003 |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/science/nature/2956925.stm |work=BBC News }}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Damaidi]] around 6000&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}},&lt;ref&gt;[[Qiu Xigui]] (2000). ''Chinese Writing''. English translation of æ–‡å­—å­¸æ¦‚è«– by Gilbert L. Mattos and [[Jerry Norman (sinologist)|Jerry Norman]]. Early China Special Monograph Series No. 4. Berkeley: The Society for the Study of Early China and the Institute of East Asian Studies, University of California, Berkeley. ISBN 978-1-55729-071-7.&lt;/ref&gt; [[Dadiwan culture|Dadiwan]] from 5800â€“5400&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}}, and [[Banpo]] dating from the 5th millennium&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}}. Some scholars have suggested that the [[Jiahu symbols]] (7th millennium&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}}) constituted the earliest Chinese writing system.&lt;ref name= &quot;earliest writing&quot;/&gt;

===Early dynastic rule===
{{Further|Dynasties in Chinese history}}
[[File:Yinxu.jpg|thumb|left|200px|[[Yinxu]], the ruins of an palace ascribed to the [[Shang Dynasty]] (14th century&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}})]]
According to Chinese tradition, the [[list of Chinese dynasties|first dynasty]] was the [[Xia dynasty|Xia]], which emerged around 2100&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}}.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|last=Tanner|first=Harold M.|title=China: A History|year=2009|publisher=Hackett Publishing|pages=35â€“36|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=VIWC9wCX2c8C&amp;pg=PA35#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|isbn=0872209156}}&lt;/ref&gt; The dynasty was considered [[Chinese mythology|mythical]] by historians until scientific excavations found early [[Bronze Age]] sites at [[Erlitou culture|Erlitou]], Henan in 1959.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.nga.gov/exhibitions/chbro_bron.shtm &quot;Bronze Age China&quot;]. [[National Gallery of Art]]. Retrieved 11 July 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; It remains unclear whether these sites are the remains of the [[Xia dynasty]] or of another culture from the same period.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|title=China: Five Thousand Years of History and Civilization|year=2007|publisher=City University of HK Press|page=25|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=z-fAxn_9f8wC&amp;pg=PA25#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|isbn=9789629371401}}&lt;/ref&gt; The succeeding [[Shang dynasty]] is the earliest to be confirmed by contemporary records.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|last=Pletcher|first=Kenneth|title=The History of China|year=2011|publisher=Britannica Educational Publishing|page=35|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=A1nwvKNPMWkC&amp;pg=PA35#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|isbn=9781615301812}}&lt;/ref&gt; The Shang ruled the plain of the [[Yellow River]] in eastern China from the 17th to the 11th century&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}}.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|last1=Fowler|first1=Jeaneane D. |first2=Merv |last2=Fowler |title=Chinese Religions: Beliefs and Practices|year=2008|publisher=Sussex Academic Press|page=17|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=rpJNfIAZltoC&amp;pg=PA17#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|isbn=9781845191726}}&lt;/ref&gt; Their [[oracle bone script]] (from {{c.|1500}}&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}}) &lt;ref&gt;William G. Boltz, Early Chinese Writing, World Archaeology, Vol. 17, No. 3, Early Writing Systems. (Feb. 1986), pp. 420â€“436 (436).&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;David N. Keightley, &quot;Art, Ancestors, and the Origins of Writing in China&quot;, ''Representations'', No. 56, Special Issue: The New Erudition. (Autumn, 1996), pp.68â€“95 (68).&lt;/ref&gt; represents the oldest form of Chinese writing yet found,&lt;ref&gt;{{cite encyclopedia| url=https://books.google.com/books?id=vWLRxJEU49EC&amp;pg=PA904#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false | page=904 | first=Pam |last=Hollister |title=Zhengzhou | encyclopedia=International Dictionary of Historic Places: Asia and Oceania |editor1-first=Paul E. |editor1-last=Schellinger |editor2-first=Robert M. |editor2-last= Salkin |publisher= Fitzroy Dearborn Publishers |year=1996| isbn=9781884964046}}&lt;/ref&gt; and is a direct ancestor of modern [[Chinese characters]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|last=Allan|first=Keith|title=The Oxford Handbook of the History of Linguistics|year=2013|publisher=Oxford University Press|page=4|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=BzfRFmlN2ZAC&amp;pg=PA4#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|isbn=9780199585847}}&lt;/ref&gt; The Shang were conquered by the [[Zhou dynasty|Zhou]], who ruled between the 11th and 5th centuries&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}}, though centralized authority was slowly eroded by feudal warlords. Many independent states eventually emerged from the weakened Zhou state and continually waged war with each other in the 300-year [[Spring and Autumn Period]], only occasionally deferring to the Zhou king. By the time of the [[Warring States period]] of the 5thâ€“3rd centuries&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}}, there were seven powerful sovereign states in what is now China, each with its own king, ministry and army.

===Imperial China===
[[File:Chinesische-mauer.jpg|thumb|left|200px|China's [[First Emperor]] is famed for having united the [[Warring States]]' barriers to form the first [[Great Wall of China]]. Most of the present structure, however, dates to the [[Ming Dynasty]].]]
[[File:Terracotta pmorgan.jpg|thumb|left|200px|The [[Terracotta Army]] ({{c.|210}}&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}}) discovered outside the [[Mausoleum of the First Qin Emperor|tomb]] of the [[First Emperor]] in modern [[Xi'an]].]]
The [[Warring States]] period ended in 221&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}} after the [[state of Qin]] conquered the other six kingdoms and established the first unified Chinese state. Its [[King Zheng of Qin|King&amp;nbsp;Zheng]] proclaimed himself the [[First Emperor]] of the [[Qin Dynasty]] (''QÃ­n ShÇ?huÃ¡ng'' or ''ShÇ? HuÃ¡ngdÃ¬''). He enacted Qin's [[Legalism (philosophy)|legalist]] reforms throughout China, notably the forced standardization of [[Chinese characters]], [[Chinese units|measurements]], road widths (i.e., cart axles' length), and [[history of Chinese currency|currency]]. His dynasty also [[Qin's campaign against the Yue tribes|conquered the Yue tribes]] in [[Guangxi]], [[Guangdong]], and northern [[Vietnam]].&lt;ref&gt;Sima Qian, Translated by Burton Watson. ''Records of the Grand Historian: Han Dynasty I'', p. 11-12. ISBN 0-231-08165-0.&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Qin dynasty]] lasted only fifteen years, falling soon after the First Emperor's death, as his harsh authoritarian policies led to widespread rebellion.&lt;ref name=&quot;Bodde1986&quot;&gt;Bodde, Derk. (1986). &quot;The State and Empire of Ch'in&quot;, in ''The Cambridge History of China: Volume I: the Ch'in and Han Empires, 221 B.C. â€“ A.D. 220''. Edited by Denis Twitchett and Michael Loewe. Cambridge: Cambridge University Press. ISBN 0-521-24327-0.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Lewis2007&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han|first=Mark Edward|last=Lewis|publisher=Belknap Press|location=London|year=2007|isbn=978-0-674-02477-9}}&lt;/ref&gt;

Following a [[Chu-Han contention|widespread civil war]] during which the imperial library at [[Xianyang]] [[List of destroyed libraries#Human action|was burned]],{{efn|Owing to the [[First Emperor]]'s earlier policy involving the &quot;[[burning of books and burying of scholars]]&quot;, the destruction of the confiscated copies at Xianyang was an event similar to the [[destruction of the Library of Alexandria|destructions]] of the [[Library of Alexandria]] in the west. Even those texts that did survive had to be painstakingly reconstructed from memory, luck, or forgery.&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |last=Cotterell |first=Arthur |title=The Imperial Capitals of China |pp=[https://books.google.com.hk/books?id=bZI764AEfcsC&amp;pg=PA35 35â€“36] |publisher=Pimlico |date=2011 }}.&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Old Texts]] of the [[Five Classics]] were said to have been found hidden in a wall at the Kong residence in [[Qufu]]. [[Mei Ze]]'s &quot;rediscovered&quot; edition of the [[Book of Documents]] was [[Yan Ruoqu|only shown to be a forgery in the Qing dynasty]].}} the [[Han dynasty]] emerged to rule China between 206&amp;nbsp;{{sc|bc}} and {{sc|ad}}&amp;nbsp;220, creating a cultural identity among its populace still remembered in the ethnonym of the [[Han Chinese]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Bodde1986&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Lewis2007&quot; /&gt; The Han [[History of the Han Dynasty|expanded the empire's territory considerably]], with military campaigns reaching [[Hanâ€“Xiongnu War|Central Asia, Mongolia]], [[Gojoseonâ€“Han War|Korea]], and [[Han campaigns against Dian|Yunnan]], and the [[Southward expansion of the Han dynasty|recovery of Guangdong and northern Vietnam]] from [[Nanyue]]. Han involvement in Central Asia helped establish the land route of the [[Silk Road]], replacing the earlier path over the [[Himalayas]] to India. Han China gradually became the largest economy of the ancient world.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.eric.ed.gov/ERICWebPortal/custom/portlets/recordDetails/detailmini.jsp?_nfpb=true&amp;_&amp;ERICExtSearch_SearchValue_0=ED460052&amp;ERICExtSearch_SearchType_0=no&amp;accno=ED460052 |title=Dahlman, Carl J; Aubert, Jean-Eric. ''China and the Knowledge Economy: Seizing the 21st century''|publisher=World Bank Publications via Eric.ed.gov|accessdate=22 October 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Despite the Han's initial decentralization and the official abandonment of the Qin philosophy of [[Legalism (philosophy)|Legalism]] in favor of [[Confucianism]], Qin's legalist institutions and policies continued to be employed by the Han government and its successors.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|first1=Candice |last1=Goucher |first2= Linda |last2=Walton|title=World History: Journeys from Past to Present â€“ Volume 1: From Human Origins to 1500 CE|year=2013|publisher=Routledge|page=108|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=zdwpAAAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA108 |isbn=9781135088224}}&lt;/ref&gt;

After the [[End of the Han dynasty|collapse of Han]], a period of strife known as [[Three Kingdoms]] followed,&lt;ref&gt;Whiting, Marvin C. (2002). ''Imperial Chinese Military History.'' iUniverse. p. 214&lt;/ref&gt; whose central figures were later immortalized in [[Romance of the Three Kingdoms|one]] of the [[Four Classics]] of [[Chinese literature]]. At its end, [[Cao Wei|Wei]] was swiftly overthrown by the [[Jin dynasty (265â€“420)|Jin dynasty]]. The Jin fell to [[War of the Eight Princes|civil war]] upon the ascension of a [[Emperor Hui of Jin|developmentally-disabled emperor]]; the [[Five Barbarians]] then [[uprising of the Five Barbarians|invaded]] and ruled northern China as the [[Sixteen Kingdoms]]. The [[Xianbei]] unified them as the [[Northern Wei]], whose [[Emperor Xiaowen of Northern Wei|Emperor Xiaowen]] reversed his predecessors' apartheid policies and [[Northern and Southern dynasties#Northern dynasties|enforced a drastic sinification on his subjects]], largely integrating them into Chinese culture. In the south, the general [[Liu Yu]] secured the abdication of the Jin in favor of the [[Liu Song]]. The various successors of these states became known as the [[Northern and Southern dynasties]], with the two areas finally reunited by the [[Sui Dynasty|Sui]] in 581. The Sui restored the Han to power through China, reformed its agriculture and economy, constructed the [[Grand Canal of China|Grand Canal]], and patronized [[Buddhism in China|Buddhism]]. However, they fell quickly when their conscription for public works and a [[Goguryeoâ€“Sui War|failed war]] with [[Goguryeo|Korea]] provoked widespread unrest.&lt;ref&gt;Ki-Baik Lee (1984). ''A new history of Korea.'' Harvard University Press. ISBN 978-0-674-61576-2. p.47.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;David Andrew Graff (2002). ''Medieval Chinese warfare, 300â€“900.'' Routledge. ISBN 0-415-23955-9. p.13.&lt;/ref&gt;

[[File:Along the River During the Qingming Festival (detail of original).jpg|200px|thumb|A detail from ''[[Along the River During the Qingming Festival]]'', a 12th-century painting showing everyday life in the [[Song dynasty]]'s capital, [[Bianjing]] (present-day [[Kaifeng]])]]
Under the succeeding [[Tang dynasty|Tang]] and [[Song dynasty|Song]] dynasties, Chinese economy, technology, and culture entered a golden age.&lt;ref&gt;Adshead, S. A. M. (2004). ''T'ang China: The Rise of the East in World History''. New York: Palgrave Macmillan. p. 54&lt;/ref&gt; The Tang Empire returned control of the [[Western Regions]] and the Silk Road,&lt;ref&gt;{{citation|last=Nishijima|first=Sadao|editor1-last=Twitchett|editor1-first=Denis|editor2-last=Loewe|editor2-first=Michael|chapter=The Economic and Social History of Former Han|title=Cambridge History of China: Volume I: the Ch'in and Han Empires, 221 B.C. â€“ A.D. 220|year=1986|publisher=Cambridge University Press|location=Cambridge|isbn=0-521-24327-0|pages=545â€“607}}&lt;/ref&gt; and made the capital [[Chang'an]] a cosmopolitan urban center. However, it was devastated and weakened by the [[An Shi Rebellion]] in the 8th century.&lt;ref&gt;City University of HK Press (2007). ''China: Five Thousand Years of History and Civilization''. ISBN 962-937-140-5. p.71&lt;/ref&gt; In 907, the Tang disintegrated completely with the local military governors being ungovernable. Finally the Song Dynasty ended the [[Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms period|separatist situation]], as a result the balance of power appeared between Song and [[Khitan Liao]]. The Song was the first government in world history to issue paper money and the first Chinese [[polity]] to establish a permanent standing navy which was supported by the developed shipbuilding industry along with the sea trade.&lt;ref&gt;Paludan, Ann (1998). ''Chronicle of the Chinese Emperors''. London: Thames &amp; Hudson. ISBN 0-500-05090-2. p. 136.&lt;/ref&gt; Between the 10th and 11th centuries, the population of China doubled in size to around 100 million people, mostly because of the expansion of rice cultivation in central and southern China, and the production of abundant food surpluses. The Song dynasty also saw a [[Neo-Confucianism|revival of Confucianism]], in response to the growth of Buddhism during the Tang,&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|title=Essentials of Neo-Confucianism: Eight Major Philosophers of the Song and Ming Periods|year=1999|publisher=Greenwood Publishing Group|page=3|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=sjzPPg8eK7sC&amp;pg=PA3#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|isbn=9780313264498}}&lt;/ref&gt; and a flourishing of philosophy and the arts, as [[landscape art]] and [[porcelain]] were brought to new levels of maturity and complexity.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Northern Song dynasty (960â€“1127)|url=http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/hd/nsong/hd_nsong.htm|publisher=Metropolitan Museum of Art|accessdate=27 November 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[http://d.wanfangdata.com.cn/Thesis_Y1795153.aspx &quot;ä»Žæ±?çª‘ã€?ä¿®å†…å?¸çª‘å’ŒéƒŠå?›çª‘çš„æŠ€æœ¯ä¼ æ‰¿çœ‹å®‹ä»£ç“·ä¸šçš„å?‘å±•&quot;]. wanfangdata.com.cn. 15 February 2011. Retrieved 15 August 2015.&lt;/ref&gt; However, the military weakness of the Song army was observed by the [[Jurchen people|Jurchen]] [[Jin dynasty (1115â€“1234)|Jin dynasty]]. In 1127, [[Emperor Huizong of Song]] and the capital [[Bianjing]] were captured during the [[Jinâ€“Song Wars]], remnants of the Song retreated to [[Northern and southern China|southern China]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|title=Daily Life in China on the Eve of the Mongol Invasion, 1250â€“1276|year=1962|publisher=Stanford University Press|page=22|url=|isbn=0-8047-0720-0}}&lt;/ref&gt;

In the 13th century, China was [[Mongol invasion of China|gradually conquered by the Mongol Empire]]. In 1271, the [[Mongols|Mongol]] leader [[Kublai Khan]] established the [[Yuan dynasty]]; the Yuan conquered the last remnant of the Song dynasty in 1279. Before the Mongol invasion, the population of Song China was 120 million citizens; this was reduced to 60 million by the time of the census in 1300.&lt;ref&gt;Ping-ti Ho. &quot;An Estimate of the Total Population of Sung-Chin China&quot;, in ''Ã‰tudes Song'', Series 1, No 1, (1970). pp. 33â€“53.&lt;/ref&gt; A peasant named [[Zhu Yuanzhang]] overthrew the Yuan Dynasty in 1368 and founded the [[Ming dynasty]]. Under the Ming Dynasty, China enjoyed another golden age, developing one of the strongest navies in the world and a rich and prosperous economy amid a flourishing of art and culture. It was during this period that [[Zheng He]] led [[Treasure voyages|voyages throughout the world, reaching as far as Africa]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news| url=http://www.guardian.co.uk/world/2010/jul/25/kenya-china | work=The Guardian | first=Xan | last=Rice | title=Chinese archaeologists' African quest for sunken ship of Ming admiral | date=25 July 2010 | location=London}}&lt;/ref&gt; In the early years of the Ming Dynasty, China's capital was moved from [[Nanjing]] to Beijing. With the budding of capitalism, philosophers such as [[Wang Yangming]] further critiqued and expanded Neo-Confucianism with concepts of [[individualism]] and equality of [[four occupations]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Wang Yangming (1472â€”1529)|url=http://www.iep.utm.edu/wangyang/|work=Internet Encyclopedia of Philosophy|accessdate=9 December 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[scholar-official]] stratum became a supporting force of industry and commerce in the tax boycott movements, which, together with the famines and the wars against [[Japanese invasions of Korea (1592â€“1598)|Japanese invasions of Korea]] and [[Qing conquest of the Ming|Manchu invasions]] led to an exhausted treasury.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.docin.com/p-378667223.html &quot;è®ºæ˜Žæœ«å£«äººé˜¶å±‚ä¸Žèµ„æœ¬ä¸»ä¹‰è?ŒèŠ½çš„å…³ç³»&quot;]. docin.com. 8 April 2012. Retrieved 2 September 2015.&lt;/ref&gt;

In 1644, Beijing was captured by a coalition of peasant rebel forces led by [[Li Zicheng]]. The last Ming [[Chongzhen Emperor]] committed suicide when the city fell. The Manchu [[Qing dynasty]] then allied with Ming dynasty general [[Wu Sangui]] and overthrew Li's short-lived [[Shun dynasty]], and subsequently seized control of Beijing, which became the new capital of the Qing Dynasty.

===End of dynastic rule===
[[File:Regaining the Provincial Capital of Ruizhou.jpg|thumb|left|200px|A 19th-century depiction of the [[Taiping Rebellion]] (1850â€“1864).]]
The [[Qing dynasty]], which lasted from 1644 until 1912, was the last imperial dynasty of China. As a [[Conquest Dynasties|conquest dynasty]], it successively conquered the [[South Ming|Ming loyalists]] and [[Dzungar Khanate]], adding Mongolia, Tibet and Xinjiang into the empire,&lt;ref&gt;The Cambridge History of China: Volume 10, Part 1, by John K. Fairbank, p37&lt;/ref&gt; and strengthened the centralized autocracy to crackdown on [[anti-Qing sentiment]]. The policy of valuing agriculture and restraining commerce, the ''[[Haijin]]'' (&quot;sea ban&quot;) and the ideological control as represented by the [[literary inquisition]] caused social and technological stagnation.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|title=ä¸­å›½é€šå?²Â·æ˜Žæ¸…å?²|year=2010|publisher=ä¹?å·žå‡ºç‰ˆç¤¾|pages=104â€“112|url=|isbn=978-7-5108-0062-7}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|title=ä¸­å?Žé€šå?²Â·ç¬¬å??å?·|year=1996|publisher=èŠ±åŸŽå‡ºç‰ˆç¤¾|page=71|url=|isbn=978-7-5360-2320-8}}&lt;/ref&gt; In the 19th century, the dynasty experienced Western imperialism following the [[First Opium War]] (1839â€“42) and the [[Second Opium War]] (1856â€“60) with Britain and France. China was forced to sign unequal treaties, pay compensation, open treaty ports, allow extraterritoriality for foreign nationals, and cede [[Hong Kong]] to the British&lt;ref&gt;[[Ainslie Thomas Embree]], [[Carol Gluck]] (1997). ''[https://books.google.com/books?id=Xn-6yMhAungC&amp;pg=&amp;dq&amp;hl=en#v=onepage&amp;q=&amp;f=false Asia in Western and World History: A Guide for Teaching]''. M.E. Sharpe. p.597. ISBN 1-56324-265-6.&lt;/ref&gt; under the 1842 [[Treaty of Nanking]]. The [[First Sino-Japanese War]] (1894â€“95) resulted in Qing China's loss of influence in the [[Korean Peninsula]], as well as the cession of Taiwan to [[Japan]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/546176/Sino-Japanese-War|title=Sino-Japanese War (1894â€“95)|work=[[EncyclopÃ¦dia Britannica]]|accessdate=12 November 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[File:EightNationsCrime02.jpg|thumb|The [[Eight-Nation Alliance]] invaded China to defeat the anti-foreign [[Boxer Rebellion|Boxers]] and their Qing backers.]]
The Qing dynasty also began experiencing internal unrest in which millions of people died. In the 1850s and 1860s, the failed [[Taiping Rebellion]] ravaged southern China. [[Timeline of late anti-Qing rebellions|Other major rebellions]] included the [[Punti-Hakka Clan Wars]] (1855â€“67), the [[Nian Rebellion]] (1851â€“68), the [[Miao Rebellion (1854â€“73)|Miao Rebellion]] (1854â€“73), the [[Panthay Rebellion]] (1856â€“73) and the [[Dungan Revolt (1862â€“77)|Dungan Revolt]] (1862â€“77). The initial success of the [[Self-Strengthening Movement]] of the 1860s was frustrated by the series of military defeats in the 1880s and 1890s.

In the 19th century, the great [[Chinese emigration|Chinese Diaspora]] began. Losses due to emigration were added to by conflicts and catastrophes such as the [[Northern Chinese Famine of 1876â€“79]], in which between 9 and 13 million people died.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.fao.org/docrep/U8480E/U8480E05.htm &quot;Dimensions of need â€“ People and populations at risk&quot;]. 1995. Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations (FAO). Retrieved 3 July 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; In 1898, the [[Guangxu Emperor]] drafted a [[Hundred Days' Reform|reform plan]] to establish a modern [[constitutional monarchy]], but these plans were thwarted by the [[Empress Dowager Cixi]]. The ill-fated anti-Western [[Boxer Rebellion]] of 1899â€“1901 further weakened the dynasty. Although Cixi sponsored a program of reforms, the [[Xinhai Revolution]] of 1911â€“12 brought an end to the Qing dynasty and established the [[Republic of China (1912â€“49)|Republic of China]].

===Republic of China (1912â€“49)===
{{main article|Republic of China (1912â€“49)|History of the Republic of China}}
{{See also|Taiwan|Taiwan after World War II}}
[[File:Sun Yat-sen and Chiang Kai-shek.jpg|thumb|200px|[[Sun Yat-sen]], the father of modern China (seated on right), and [[Chiang Kai-shek]], later [[President of the Republic of China]]]]
[[File:1945 Mao and Chiang.jpg|thumb|200px|[[Chiang Kai-shek]] and [[Mao Zedong]] toasting together in 1946 following the end of World War II]]
On 1 January 1912, the Republic of China was established, and [[Sun Yat-sen]] of the [[Kuomintang]] (the KMT or Nationalist Party) was proclaimed provisional president.&lt;ref&gt;Eileen Tamura (1997). ''China: Understanding Its Past.'' Volume 1. University of Hawaii Press. ISBN 0-8248-1923-3. p.146.&lt;/ref&gt; However, the presidency was later given to [[Yuan Shikai]], a former Qing general who in 1915 proclaimed himself [[Empire of China (1915â€“1916)|Emperor of China]]. In the face of popular condemnation and opposition from his own [[Beiyang Army]], he was forced to abdicate and reestablish the republic.&lt;ref&gt;[[Stephen G. Haw|Stephen Haw]], (2006). Beijing: A Concise History. Taylor &amp; Francis, ISBN 0-415-39906-8. p.143.&lt;/ref&gt;

After Yuan Shikai's death in 1916, China was politically fragmented. Its Beijing-based government was internationally recognized but virtually powerless; regional warlords controlled most of its territory.&lt;ref&gt;Bruce Elleman (2001). ''Modern Chinese Warfare''. Routledge. ISBN 0-415-21474-2. p.149.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Graham Hutchings (2003). ''Modern China: A Guide to a Century of Change''. Harvard University Press. ISBN 0-674-01240-2. p.459.&lt;/ref&gt; In the late 1920s, the Kuomintang, under [[Chiang Kai-shek]], the then Principal of the [[Republic of China Military Academy]], was able to reunify the country under its own control with a series of deft military and political manoeuvrings, known collectively as the [[Northern Expedition]].&lt;ref&gt;Peter Zarrow (2005). ''China in War and Revolution, 1895â€“1949''. Routledge. ISBN 0-415-36447-7. p.230.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;M. Leutner (2002). ''The Chinese Revolution in the 1920s: Between Triumph and Disaster''. Routledge. ISBN 0-7007-1690-4. p.129.&lt;/ref&gt; The Kuomintang moved the nation's capital to [[Nanjing]] and implemented &quot;political tutelage&quot;, an intermediate stage of political development outlined in Sun Yat-sen's [[San-min Doctrine|San-min]] program for transforming China into a modern democratic state.&lt;ref&gt;Hung-Mao Tien (1972). ''Government and Politics in Kuomintang China, 1927â€“1937 (Volume 53)''. Stanford University Press. ISBN 0-8047-0812-6. pp.&amp;nbsp;60â€“72.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Suisheng Zhao (2000). ''China and Democracy: Reconsidering the Prospects for a Democratic China''. Routledge. ISBN 0-415-92694-7. p.43.&lt;/ref&gt; The [[List of warlords and military cliques in the Warlord Era|political division]] in China made it difficult for Chiang to battle the [[Chinese Communists|Communists]], against whom the Kuomintang had been warring since 1927 in the [[Chinese Civil War]]. This war continued successfully for the Kuomintang, especially after the Communists retreated in the [[Long March]], until Japanese aggression and the 1936 [[Xi'an Incident]] forced Chiang to confront [[Imperial Japan]].&lt;ref&gt;David Ernest Apter, Tony Saich (1994). ''Revolutionary Discourse in Mao's Republic''. Harvard University Press. ISBN 0-674-76780-2. p.198.&lt;/ref&gt;

The [[Second Sino-Japanese War]] (1937â€“1945), a [[Theater (warfare)|theater]] of [[World War II]], forced an uneasy alliance between the Kuomintang and the Communists. Japanese forces committed numerous [[Japanese war crimes|war atrocities]] against the civilian population; in all, as many as 20 million Chinese civilians died.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.bbc.co.uk/history/worldwars/wwtwo/nuclear_01.shtml &quot;Nuclear Power: The End of the War Against Japan&quot;]. BBC&amp;nbsp;â€” History. Retrieved 14 July 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; An estimated 200,000 Chinese [[Nanking Massacre|were massacred]] in the city of Nanjing alone during the Japanese occupation.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.ibiblio.org/hyperwar/PTO/IMTFE/IMTFE-8.html &quot;Judgement: International Military Tribunal for the Far East&quot;]. ''Chapter VIII: Conventional War Crimes (Atrocities).'' November 1948. Retrieved 4 February 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; During the war, China, along with the UK, the US and the Soviet Union, were referred to as &quot;trusteeship of the powerful&quot; &lt;ref name = Justus&gt;{{cite book |last1=Doenecke |first1=Justus D. |last2=Stoler |first2=Mark A. |title=Debating Franklin D. Roosevelt's foreign policies, 1933â€“1945 |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=xdMF9rX6mX8C&amp;pg=PA62 |year=2005 |publisher=Rowman &amp; Littlefield }}&lt;/ref&gt; and were recognized as the Allied &quot;[[Four Policemen|Big Four]]&quot; in the [[Declaration by United Nations]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|title=Yearbook of the United Nations 1946â€“1947|date=1947|publisher=United Nations|location=Lake Success, NY|oclc=243471225|page=3|url=http://www.unmultimedia.org/searchers/yearbook/page.jsp?volume=1946-47&amp;page=38|accessdate=25 April 2015|chapter=The Moscow Declaration on general security}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.un.org/en/aboutun/history/declaration.shtml|title=Declaration by United Nations|publisher=United Nations|accessdate=20 June 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; Along with the other three great powers, China was one of the four major [[Allies of World War II]], and was later considered one of the primary victors in the war.&lt;ref&gt;Hoopes, Townsend, and Douglas Brinkley. ''FDR and the Creation of the U.N.'' (Yale University Press, 1997)&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|first=John Lewis|last=Gaddis|title=The United States and the Origins of the Cold War, 1941â€“1947|year=1972|publisher=Columbia University Press|isbn=978-0-231-12239-9|pages=24â€“25|ref=harv}}&lt;/ref&gt; After the [[surrender of Japan]] in 1945, Taiwan, including the [[Pescadores]], was returned to Chinese control. China emerged victorious but war-ravaged and financially drained. The continued distrust between the Kuomintang and the Communists led to the resumption of civil war. In 1947, constitutional rule was established, but because of the ongoing unrest, many provisions of the [[Constitution of the Republic of China|ROC constitution]] were never implemented in mainland China.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite encyclopedia|encyclopedia=Constitutional Reform and the Future of the Republic of China|year=1991|publisher=M.E. Sharpe|page=3|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=xCxMn-2msr8C&amp;pg=PA3 |first=Hung-mao |last=Tien |editor-first= Harvey |editor-last=Feldman|title=Constitutional Reform and the Future of the Republic of China|isbn=9780873328807}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===People's Republic of China (1949â€“present)===
{{Main article|History of the People's Republic of China}}
[[File:Mao proclaiming the establishment of the PRC in 1949.jpg|thumb|200px|[[Mao Zedong]] proclaiming the establishment of the PRC in 1949]]

Major combat in the [[Chinese Civil War]] ended in 1949 with the Communist Party in control of most of [[mainland China]], and the Kuomintang retreating offshore, reducing the ROC's territory to only [[Taiwan (island)|Taiwan]], [[Hainan]], and their surrounding islands. On 1 October 1949, [[Chairman of the Communist Party of China|Communist Party Chairman]] [[Mao Zedong]] proclaimed the establishment of the People's Republic of China.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_c&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.isop.ucla.edu/eas/documents/mao490921.htm|title=The Chinese people have stood up|publisher=UCLA Center for East Asian Studies|accessdate= 16 April 2006|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20090218071231/http://www.international.ucla.edu/eas/documents/mao490921.htm|archivedate=18 February 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; In 1950, the [[People's Liberation Army]] succeeded in [[Landing Operation on Hainan Island|capturing Hainan]] from the ROC&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=https://news.google.com/newspapers?nid=1817&amp;dat=19500509&amp;id=FUw_AAAAIBAJ&amp;sjid=skwMAAAAIBAJ&amp;pg=3627,3301880|title=Red Capture of Hainan Island|newspaper=The Tuscaloosa News|publisher=Google News Archive|date=9 May 1950|accessdate=20 July 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; and [[Incorporation of Tibet into the People's Republic of China|incorporating Tibet]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.usc.edu/dept/LAS/ir/cews/database/Tibet/tibet.pdf|title=The Tibetans|publisher=University of Southern California|accessdate=20 July 2013|format=PDF}}&lt;/ref&gt; However, remaining Nationalist forces continued to wage [[Kuomintang Islamic insurgency in China (1950â€“58)|an insurgency in western China]] throughout the 1950s.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|url=https://books.google.com/?id=ZNCghCIbyVAC&amp;pg=PA169&amp;dq=C.I.A++Ma+bufang#v=onepage&amp;q=C.I.A%20%20Ma%20bufang&amp;f=false|title=The Sino-American alliance: Nationalist China and American Cold War strategy in Asia|author=John W. Garver|year=1997|publisher=M.E. Sharpe|location=|isbn=0-7656-0025-0|page=169|pages=|accessdate=20 July 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

Mao's regime consolidated its popularity among the peasants through the land reform with between 1 and 2 million [[landlord]]s executed.&lt;ref&gt;Busky, Donald F. (2002). ''[https://books.google.com/books?id=Q6b0j1VINWgC&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;source=gbs_navlinks_s#v=onepage&amp;q=&amp;f=false Communism in History and Theory]''. Greenwood Publishing Group. p.11.&lt;/ref&gt; Under its leadership, China developed an independent industrial system and its own nuclear weapons.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title= A Country Study: China |url=http://lcweb2.loc.gov/frd/cs/cntoc.html|website=lcweb2.loc.gov|accessdate=24 July 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; The Chinese population almost doubled from around 550 million to over 900 million.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite book|author=Madelyn Holmes |url=https://books.google.com/?id=lJK-GRriJAoC&amp;pg=&amp;dq#v=onepage&amp;q=&amp;f=false |title=Students and teachers of the new China: thirteen interviews |publisher=McFarland |accessdate=7 November 2011 | year=2008 |page=185 |isbn= 0-7864-3288-8}}&lt;/ref&gt; However, Mao's [[Great Leap Forward]], a large-scale economic and social reform project, resulted in [[Great Chinese Famine|an estimated 45&amp;nbsp;million deaths]] between 1958 and 1961, mostly from starvation.&lt;ref name=&quot;Akbar2010&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url =http://www.independent.co.uk/arts-entertainment/books/news/maos-great-leap-forward-killed-45-million-in-four-years-2081630.html |title= Mao's Great Leap Forward 'killed 45 million in four years'|accessdate=30 October 2010 |work=The Independent |location=London |first=Arifa|last=Akbar|date=17 September 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; In 1966, Mao and his allies launched the [[Cultural Revolution]], sparking a decade of political recrimination and social upheaval which lasted until Mao's death in 1976. In October 1971, the PRC [[United Nations General Assembly Resolution 2758|replaced the Republic of China]] in the United Nations, and took its seat as a permanent member of the Security Council.&lt;ref&gt;Michael Y.M. Kao. &quot;Taiwan's and Beijing's Campaigns for Unification&quot; in Harvey Feldman and Michael Y. M. Kao (eds., 1988): ''Taiwan in a Time of Transition''. New York: Paragon House. p.188.&lt;/ref&gt;

In 1976, Mao died. The [[Gang of Four]] was quickly arrested and held responsible for the excesses of the Cultural Revolution. In 1978 [[Deng Xiaoping]] took power and instituted significant [[Chinese economic reform|economic reforms]]. The Communist Party loosened governmental control over citizens' personal lives, and the [[People's commune|communes]] were gradually disbanded in favor of private land leases. This marked China's transition from a planned economy to a mixed economy with an [[socialism with Chinese characteristics|increasingly open market environment]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_e&quot;&gt;Hart-Landsberg, Martin; and Burkett, Paul. [http://www.monthlyreview.org/chinaandsocialism.htm &quot;China and Socialism: Market Reforms and Class Struggle&quot;]. Monthly Review. Retrieved 30 October 2008.&lt;/ref&gt; China adopted its current [[constitution of the People's Republic of China|constitution]] on 4 December 1982. In 1989, the [[People's Liberation Army at Tiananmen Square protests of 1989|violent suppression]] of [[Tiananmen Square protests of 1989|student protests in Tiananmen Square]] brought condemnation and sanctions against the Chinese government from various countries.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=The Impact of Tiananmen on China's Foreign Policy|url=http://www.nbr.org/publications/element.aspx?id=73#.UpeNH_lciaU|publisher=The National Bureau of Asian Research|accessdate=28 November 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[Jiang Zemin]], [[Li Peng]] and [[Zhu Rongji]] led the nation in the 1990s. Under their administration, China's economic performance pulled an estimated 150&amp;nbsp;million peasants out of poverty and sustained an average annual gross domestic product growth rate of 11.2%.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_h&quot;&gt;[http://www.chinadaily.com.cn/en/doc/2003-07/11/content_244499.htm ''Nation bucks trend of global poverty'']. ''China Daily''. 11 July 2003. Retrieved 10 July 2013.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_i&quot;&gt;[http://english.people.com.cn/english/200003/01/eng20000301X115.html ''China's Average Economic Growth in 90s Ranked 1st in World'']. ''People's Daily''. 1 March 2000. Retrieved 10 July 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; The country formally joined the [[World Trade Organization]] in 2001, and maintained its high rate of economic growth under [[Hu Jintao]] and [[Wen Jiabao]]'s leadership in the 2000s. However, rapid growth also severely impacted the country's resources and environment,&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2007/08/26/world/asia/20070826_CHINA_GRAPHIC.html#|title=China's Environmental Crisis|work=[[New York Times]]|date=26 August 2007|accessdate=16 May 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_j&quot;&gt;[http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/world/asia-pacific/4913622.stm ''China worried over pace of growth'']. BBC. Retrieved 16 April 2006.&lt;/ref&gt; and caused [[Protest and dissent in the People's Republic of China|major social displacement]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_k&quot;&gt;[http://migration.ucdavis.edu/mn/more.php?id=3166_0_3_0 ''China: Migrants, Students, Taiwan'']. Migration News. January 2006.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_l&quot;&gt;[http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-dyn/content/article/2006/01/27/AR2006012701588.html ''In Face of Rural Unrest, China Rolls Out Reforms'']. ''Washington Post''. 28 January 2006.&lt;/ref&gt; Living standards continued to improve rapidly despite the [[late-2000s recession]], but centralized political control remained tight.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.pbs.org/wgbh/pages/frontline/tankman/etc/transcript.html
| title=''Frontline'': ''The Tank Man'' transcript
| accessdate=12 July 2008 |date=11 April 2006 |work=Frontline |publisher=PBS }}&lt;/ref&gt;

Preparations for a decadal Communist Party leadership change in 2012 were marked by [[Wang Lijun incident|factional disputes and political scandals]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-17673505|title=Bo Xilai scandal: Timeline|publisher=BBC|date=5 September 2012|accessdate=11 September 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; During China's [[18th National Congress of the Communist Party of China|18th National Communist Party Congress]] in November 2012, Hu Jintao was replaced as [[General Secretary of the Communist Party of China|General Secretary of the Communist Party]] by [[Xi Jinping]].&lt;ref name=&quot;XiJinpingLiKeqiang&quot;&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/worldnews/asia/china/9679477/Xi-Jinping-crowned-new-leader-of-China-Communist-Party.html|title=Xi Jinping crowned new leader of China Communist Party|work=The Daily Telegraph|date=15 November 2012|accessdate=15 November 2012|location=London|first=Malcolm|last=Moore}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.stuff.co.nz/world/asia/7912682/New-China-leadership-tipped-to-be-all-male|title=New China leadership tipped to be all male|publisher=Stuff.co.nz|date=6 November 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Under Xi, the Chinese government began large-scale efforts to reform its economy,&lt;ref name=&quot;BBC19July2013a&quot;&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-23377060|title=China frees up bank lending rates|publisher=BBC|date=19 July 2013|accessdate=19 July 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.telegraph.co.uk/finance/china-business/10198410/China-eyes-fresh-stimulus-as-economy-stalls-sets-7pc-growth-floor.html|title=China eyes fresh stimulus as economy stalls, sets 7pc growth floor|work=Daily Telegraph|date=23 July 2013|accessdate=25 July 2013|location=London|first=Ambrose|last=Evans-Pritchard}}&lt;/ref&gt; which has suffered from structural instabilities and slowing growth.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://blogs.ft.com/gavyndavies/2012/11/25/the-decade-of-xi-jinping/#axzz2DQ0Yc2jM|title=The decade of Xi Jinping|work=[[Financial Times]]|date=25 November 2012|accessdate=27 November 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;9Dec2012&quot;&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-20657311|title=China sees both industrial output and retail sales rise|publisher=BBC|date=9 December 2012|accessdate=9 December 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-23251089|title=China's exports and imports decline|publisher=BBC|date=10 July 2013|accessdate=10 July 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-23486466|title=China orders government debt audit|publisher=BBC|date=29 July 2013|accessdate=29 July 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Xiâ€“Li Administration]] also announced major reforms to the [[one-child policy]] and prison system.&lt;ref name=SlateChina2013/&gt;
