===Political geography===
{{main article|Borders of China|Territorial changes of the People's Republic of China}}
The People's Republic of China is the [[List of countries and outlying territories by land area|second-largest]] country in the world by land area&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|last=Amitendu|first=Palit|title=China-India Economics: Challenges, Competition and Collaboration|year=2012|publisher=Routledge|page=4|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=Sz12DTzuhk0C&amp;pg=PA4#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|isbn=9781136621628}}&lt;/ref&gt; after [[Russia]], and is either the [[List of countries and outlying territories by total area|third- or fourth-largest]] by total area, after Russia, Canada and, depending on the definition of total area, the United States.{{efn|According to the ''EncyclopÃ¦dia Britannica'', the total area of the United States, at {{convert|9522055|km2|sqmi|abbr=on|sp=us}}, is slightly smaller than that of China. Meanwhile, the CIA World Factbook states that China's total area was greater than that of the United States until the coastal waters of the [[Great Lakes]] was added to the United States' total area in 1996. From 1989 through 1996, the total area of US was listed as {{convert|9372610|km2|sqmi|abbr=on|sp=us}} (land area plus inland water only). The listed total area changed to {{convert|9629091|km2|sqmi|abbr=on|sp=us}} in 1997 (with the Great Lakes areas and the coastal waters added), to {{convert|9631418|km2|sqmi|abbr=on|sp=us}} in 2004, to {{convert|9631420|km2|sqmi|abbr=on|sp=us}} in 2006, and to {{convert|9826630|km2|sqmi|abbr=on|sp=us}} in 2007 (territorial waters added).}} China's total area is generally stated as being approximately {{convert|9600000|km2|sqmi|abbr=on|sp=us}}.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.china.org.cn/english/travel/229567.htm |title=Geography â€“ china.org.cn |publisher=china.org.cn |accessdate=31 May 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; Specific area figures range from {{convert|9572900|km2|sqmi|abbr=on|sp=us}} according to the ''[[EncyclopÃ¦dia Britannica]]'',&lt;ref name=&quot;archive&quot;&gt;{{cite web |url=http://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/616563/United-States |title=United States |publisher=EncyclopÃ¦dia Britannica |accessdate=25 March 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt; {{convert|9596961|km2|sqmi|abbr=on|sp=us}} according to the UN Demographic Yearbook,&lt;ref name=&quot;UN Stat&quot; /&gt; to {{convert|9596961|km2|sqmi|abbr=on|sp=us}} according to the [[CIA World Factbook]].&lt;ref name=&quot;CIA&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=https://www.cia.gov/library/publications/the-world-factbook/geos/ch.html |title=CIA â€“ The World Factbook |publisher=Cia.gov |accessdate=23 November 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

China has the [[List of land border lengths|longest combined land border in the world]], measuring  {{convert|22117|km|mi|abbr=on|sp=us}} from the mouth of the [[Yalu River]] to the [[Gulf of Tonkin]].&lt;ref name=&quot;CIA&quot;/&gt; China [[Borders of China|borders 14 nations]], [[List of countries by number of neighbouring countries|more than any other country]] except Russia, which also borders 14.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Which country borders the most other countries?|url=http://geography.about.com/library/faq/blqzmostneighbors.htm|publisher=About.com|accessdate=5 December 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; China extends across much of East Asia, bordering [[Vietnam]], [[Laos]], and [[Myanmar|Myanmar (Burma)]] in Southeast Asia; [[India]], [[Bhutan]], [[Nepal]], [[Afghanistan]], and [[Pakistan]]{{efn|China's border with Pakistan and part of its border with India falls in the disputed region of [[Kashmir]]. The area under Pakistani administration is claimed by India, while the area under Indian administration is claimed by Pakistan.}} in South Asia;  [[Tajikistan]], [[Kyrgyzstan]] and [[Kazakhstan]] in Central Asia; and [[Russia]], [[Mongolia]], and [[North Korea]] in [[Inner Asia]] and [[Northeast Asia]]. Additionally, China shares maritime boundaries with [[South Korea]], Japan, Vietnam, and the [[Philippines]].

===Landscape and climate===
{{Multiple image
 | align = left
 | direction = vertical
 | width = 200
 | image1 = Sanya Sun Photo by Dale Preston.jpg
 | caption1 = The [[South China Sea]] coast at [[Hainan]]
 | image2 =1 jiuzhaigou valley national park wu hua hai.jpg
 | caption2 = [[Jiuzhaigou Valley]] in Sichuan
}}

The territory of China lies between [[latitude]]s [[18th parallel north|18Â°]] and [[54th parallel north|54Â° N]], and [[longitude]]s [[73rd meridian east|73Â°]] and [[135th meridian east|135Â° E]]. China's landscapes vary significantly across its vast width. In the east, along the shores of the [[Yellow Sea]] and the [[East China Sea]], there are extensive and densely populated [[alluvium|alluvial plains]], while on the edges of the Inner Mongolian plateau in the north, broad [[grassland]]s predominate. Southern China is dominated by hills and low mountain ranges, while the central-east hosts the [[river delta|deltas]] of China's two major rivers, the [[Yellow River]] and the [[Yangtze River]]. Other major rivers include the [[Xi River|Xi]], [[Mekong]], [[Brahmaputra River|Brahmaputra]] and [[Amur River|Amur]]. To the west sit major mountain ranges, most notably the Himalayas. High [[plateau]]s feature among the more arid landscapes of the north, such as the [[Taklamakan Desert|Taklamakan]] and the [[Gobi Desert]]. The world's highest point, [[Mount Everest]] (8,848m), lies on the Sino-Nepalese border.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=Nepal and China agree on Mount Everest's height|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/south_asia/8608913.stm|newspaper=BBC News|date=8 April 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; The country's lowest point, and the world's third-lowest, is the dried lake bed of [[Ayding Lake]] (âˆ’154m) in the [[Turpan Depression]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Lowest Places on Earth|url=http://www.nps.gov/deva/naturescience/lowest-places-on-earth.htm|publisher=National Park Service|accessdate=2 December 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

China's climate is mainly dominated by [[dry season]]s and wet [[monsoons]], which lead to pronounced temperature differences between winter and summer. In the winter, northern winds coming from high-latitude areas are cold and dry; in summer, southern winds from coastal areas at lower latitudes are warm and moist.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|title=Regional Climate Studies of China|year=2008|publisher=Springer|page=1|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=SEO_RyNDJ0gC&amp;pg=PA1#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|isbn=9783540792420}}&lt;/ref&gt; The climate in China differs from region to region because of the country's highly complex [[topography]].

A major environmental issue in China is the continued [[desertification|expansion of its deserts]], particularly the Gobi Desert.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=Fighting Desertification|url=http://www.forbes.com/sites/terrywaghorn/2011/03/07/fighting-desertification/|newspaper=Forbes|date=7 March 2011|first=Terry|last=Waghorn}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_au&quot;&gt;[http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/world/asia-pacific/4915690.stm &quot;Beijing hit by eighth sandstorm&quot;]. BBC news. Retrieved 17 April 2006.&lt;/ref&gt; Although barrier tree lines planted since the 1970s have reduced the frequency of [[sandstorms]], prolonged drought and poor agricultural practices have resulted in [[Asian dust|dust storms]] plaguing northern China each spring, which then spread to other parts of east Asia, including Korea and Japan. China's environmental watchdog, [[Ministry of Environmental Protection of the People's Republic of China|SEPA]], stated in 2007 that China is losing a million acres (4,000&amp;nbsp;kmÂ²) per year to desertification.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_av&quot;&gt;{{cite journal | last=Coonan | first=Cliff | url=http://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/asia/the-gathering-sandstorm-encroaching-desert-missing-water-399653.html | title=The gathering sandstorm: Encroaching desert, missing water | journal=The Independent | date=9 November 2007 | accessdate=23 July 2014 | archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20080424052106/http://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/asia/the-gathering-sandstorm-encroaching-desert-missing-water-399653.html | archivedate=24 April 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt; Water quality, [[erosion]], and [[Pollution in China|pollution control]] have become important issues in China's relations with other countries. Melting [[glaciers]] in the Himalayas could potentially lead to [[water shortage]]s for hundreds of millions of people.&lt;ref name=&quot;msnbc&quot;&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.msnbc.msn.com/id/27894721/|title=Himalaya glaciers melting much faster|publisher=MSNBC|date=24 November 2008|accessdate=21 September 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Biodiversity===
{{Main article|Wildlife of China}}
[[File:Giant Panda Eating.jpg|thumb|200px|A [[giant panda]], China's most famous [[List of endangered and protected species of China|endangered]] and [[endemic]] species, at the [[Chengdu Research Base of Giant Panda Breeding]] in [[Sichuan]]]]
China is one of 17 [[megadiverse countries]],&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_2009a&quot;&gt;{{cite web |url=http://www.environment.gov.au/soe/2001/publications/theme-reports/biodiversity/biodiversity01-3.html |title=Biodiversity Theme Report |publisher=Environment.gov.au |date=10 December 2009 |accessdate=27 April 2010|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20110811045957/http://www.environment.gov.au/soe/2001/publications/theme-reports/biodiversity/biodiversity01-3.html|archivedate=11 August 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; lying in two of the world's major [[ecozone]]s: the [[Palearctic]] and the [[Indomalaya]]. By one measure, China has over 34,687 species of animals and vascular plants, making it the third-most biodiverse country in the world, after [[Brazil]] and [[Colombia]].&lt;ref&gt;[http://rainforests.mongabay.com/03highest_biodiversity.htm Countries with the Highest Biological Diversity]. Mongabay.com. 2004 data. Retrieved 24 April 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; The country signed the [[Rio de Janeiro]] [[Convention on Biological Diversity]] on 11 June 1992, and became a party to the convention on 5 January 1993.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.cbd.int/convention/parties/list|title=List of Parties |accessdate=9 December 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; It later produced a [[Biodiversity action plan|National Biodiversity Strategy and Action Plan]], with one revision that was received by the convention on 21 September 2010.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.cbd.int/doc/world/cn/cn-nbsap-v2-zh.pdf |title=[English translation: China Biodiversity Conservation Strategy and Action Plan. Years 2011â€“2030] |accessdate=9 December 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;

China is home to at least 551 species of [[List of mammals of China|mammals]] (the third-highest such number in the world),&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.iucnredlist.org/initiatives/mammals/analysis/geographic-patterns IUCN Initiatives â€“ Mammals â€“ Analysis of Data â€“ Geographic Patterns 2012]. IUCN. Retrieved 24 April 2013. Data does not include species in Taiwan.&lt;/ref&gt; 1,221 species of birds (eighth),&lt;ref&gt;[http://rainforests.mongabay.com/03birds.htm Countries with the most bird species]. Mongabay.com. 2004 data. Retrieved 24 April 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; 424 species of reptiles (seventh)&lt;ref&gt;[http://rainforests.mongabay.com/03reptiles.htm Countries with the most reptile species]. Mongabay.com. 2004 data. Retrieved 24 April 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; and 333 species of amphibians (seventh).&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.iucnredlist.org/initiatives/amphibians/analysis/geographic-patterns#diversity IUCN Initiatives â€“ Amphibians â€“ Analysis of Data â€“ Geographic Patterns 2012]. IUCN. Retrieved 24 April 2013. Data does not include species in Taiwan.&lt;/ref&gt; China is the most biodiverse country in each category outside the tropics. Wildlife in China share habitat with and bear acute pressure from the world's largest population of ''[[homo sapiens]]''. At least 840 [[List of endangered and protected species of China|animal species are threatened, vulnerable or in danger of local extinction]] in China, due mainly to human activity such as habitat destruction, pollution and poaching for food, fur and ingredients for [[traditional Chinese medicine]].&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.mnn.com/earth-matters/animals/stories/infographic-top-20-countries-with-most-endangered-species Top 20 countries with most endangered species IUCN Red List]. 5 March 2010. Retrieved 24 April 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; Endangered wildlife is protected by law, and {{As of|2005|lc=y}}, the country has over 2,349 [[Protected areas of China|nature reserves]], covering a total area of 149.95 million hectares, 15 percent of China's total land area.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Nature Reserves|url=http://www.china.org.cn/english/features/Brief/193257.htm|publisher=China.org.cn|accessdate=2 December 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

China has over 32,000 species of vascular plants,&lt;ref&gt;[http://rainforests.mongabay.com/03plants.htm Countries with the most vascular plant species]. Mongabay.com. 2004 data. Retrieved 24 April 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; and is home to a variety of forest types. Cold [[coniferous]] forests predominate in the north of the country, supporting animal species such as [[moose]] and [[Asian black bear]], along with over 120 bird species.&lt;ref name=&quot;rough guide&quot;/&gt; The understorey of moist [[conifer]] forests may contain thickets of [[bamboo]]. In higher montane stands of [[juniper]] and [[taxus|yew]], the bamboo is replaced by [[rhododendron]]s. [[Subtropical]] forests, which are predominate in central and southern China, support as many as 146,000 species of flora.&lt;ref name=&quot;rough guide&quot;/&gt; Tropical and seasonal [[rainforest]]s, though confined to [[Yunnan]] and [[Hainan Island]], contain a quarter of all the animal and plant species found in China.&lt;ref name=&quot;rough guide&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=China|year=2003|publisher=Rough Guides|page=1213|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=dA_QbQiZkB4C&amp;pg=PA1213#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|edition=3|isbn=9781843530190}}&lt;/ref&gt; China has over 10,000 recorded species of [[fungi]],&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|title=Conservation Biology: Voices from the Tropics|year=2013|publisher=John Wiley &amp; Sons|page=208|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=OeqjKhDml6wC&amp;pg=PA208#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|isbn=9781118679814}}&lt;/ref&gt; and of them, nearly 6,000 are [[higher fungi]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal|last=Liu|first=Ji-Kai|title=Secondary metabolites from higher fungi in China and their biological activity|journal=Drug Discoveries &amp; Therapeutics|year=2007|volume=1|issue=2|page=94|url=http://www.ddtjournal.com/action/downloaddoc.php?docid=57}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Environmental issues===
{{Main article|Environmental issues in China}}
{{See also|Water resources of China}}
[[File:Wind power plants in Xinjiang, China.jpg|200px|thumb|[[Wind turbine]]s in [[Xinjiang]]. The [[Dabancheng]] project is one of Asia's largest [[wind farms]]]]

In recent decades, China has suffered from [[environmental issues in China|severe environmental deterioration and pollution]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Ma2002&quot;&gt;{{Cite book |author=Ma, Xiaoying |author2=Ortalano, Leonard |title=Environmental Regulation in China |year=2000 |publisher=Rowman &amp; Littlefield Publishers |url= https://books.google.com/books?id=eQTbZRWgC74C&amp;pg=PA1#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false |page=1}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-21545868|title=China acknowledges 'cancer villages'|publisher=BBC|date=22 February 2013|accessdate=23 February 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; While regulations such as the 1979 Environmental Protection Law are fairly stringent, they are poorly enforced, as they are frequently disregarded by local communities and government officials in favor of rapid economic development.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-20114306|title=Riot police and protesters clash over China chemical plant|publisher=BBC|date=28 October 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Urban air pollution is a severe health issue in the country; the [[World Bank]] estimated in 2013 that 16 of the world's 20 most-polluted cities are located in China.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=Beijing Orders Official Cars Off Roads to Curb Pollution|url=http://www.bloomberg.com/news/2013-01-12/beijingers-told-to-stay-indoors-as-pollution-hits-record.html|accessdate=27 July 2013|publisher=[[Bloomberg L.P.]]|date=14 January 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; China is the world's largest [[carbon dioxide]] emitter.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=Global carbon emissions hit record high in 2012 |url=http://www.reuters.com/article/2013/06/10/us-iea-emissions-idUSBRE95908S20130610|accessdate=3 November 2013|publisher=Reuters|date=10 June 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; The country also has significant [[water pollution]] problems: 40% of China's rivers had been polluted by industrial and agricultural waste by late 2011.&lt;ref&gt;[http://blogs.ei.columbia.edu/2011/10/24/chinas-decade-plan-for-water/ &quot;China's decade plan for water&quot;]. The Earth Institute. [[Columbia University]]. 24 October 2011. Retrieved 23 November 2011.&lt;/ref&gt; This crisis is compounded by increasingly severe water shortages, particularly in the north-east of the country.&lt;ref name=&quot;Desalination&quot;&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-22815145|title=China works to ease water woes|publisher=BBC|date=11 June 2013|accessdate=11 June 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_2004&quot;&gt;{{Cite news |url=http://english.peopledaily.com.cn/200412/23/eng20041223_168329.html |title=300&amp;nbsp;million Chinese drinking unsafe water |date=23 December 2004 |work=People's Daily |accessdate=27 March 2009 }}&lt;/ref&gt;

However, China is the world's leading investor in [[renewable energy commercialization]], with [[US$|$]]52&amp;nbsp;billion invested in 2011 alone;&lt;ref name=&quot;By2010&quot;&gt;{{Cite news |first=Lisa|last=Friedman |url=http://www.nytimes.com/cwire/2010/03/25/25climatewire-china-leads-major-countries-with-346-billion-15729.html |title=China Leads Major Countries With $34.6&amp;nbsp;Billion Invested in Clean Technology |work=The New York Times
 |date=25 March 2010 |accessdate=27 April 2010 }}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Black2010&quot;&gt;{{Cite news |last=Black |first=Richard |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/science/nature/8587319.stm |title=China steams ahead on clean energy |publisher=BBC News |date=26 March 2010|accessdate=27 April 2010 }}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;ChinaLeadingEnergy&quot;&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.forbes.com/sites/jackperkowski/2012/07/27/china-leads-the-world-in-renewable-energy-investment/|title=China Leads The World In Renewable Energy Investment|work=[[Forbes]]|date=27 July 2012|accessdate=5 December 2012|first=Jack|last=Perkowski}}&lt;/ref&gt; it is a major manufacturer of renewable energy technologies and invests heavily in local-scale renewable energy projects.&lt;ref name=&quot;bradsher&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last=Bradsher|first=Keith|date=30 January 2010|url=http://www.nytimes.com/2010/01/31/business/energy-environment/31renew.html|title=China leads global race to make clean energy|newspaper=New York Times}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.scientificamerican.com/article.cfm?id=chinas-big-push-for-renewable-energy &quot;China's big push for renewable energy&quot;]. ''[[Scientific American]]''. 4 August 2008. Retrieved 24 September 2011.&lt;/ref&gt; By 2009, over 17% of China's energy was derived from renewable sources â€“ most notably [[hydroelectric power]] plants, of which China has a total installed capacity of 197 GW.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.ecosensorium.org/2010/11/china-tops-world-in-clean-energy.html &quot;China tops the world in clean energy production.&quot;] Ecosensorium. 2010. Retrieved 24 September 2011.&lt;/ref&gt; In 2011, the Chinese government announced plans to invest four trillion yuan (US$619 billion) in water infrastructure and [[desalination]] projects over a ten-year period, and to complete construction of a flood prevention and anti-drought system by 2020.&lt;ref name=&quot;Desalination&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.wantchinatimes.com/news-subclass-cnt.aspx?id=20110711000040&amp;cid=1105&amp;MainCatID=11 &quot;Splashing out: China to spend 4 trillion yuan on water projects&quot;]. Want China Times. 11 July 2011. Retrieved 27 November 2011.&lt;/ref&gt; In 2013, China began a five-year, US$277 billion effort to reduce air pollution, particularly in the north of the country.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=China to spend big to clean up its air|url=http://grist.org/news/china-to-spend-big-to-clean-up-its-air/?utm_campaign=daily&amp;utm_medium=email&amp;utm_source=newsletter&amp;sub_email=sundaybottle%40hotmail.com|work=Grist Magazine|accessdate=27 July 2013|first=John|last=Upton|date=25 July 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

==Politics==
{{Main article|Politics of the People's Republic of China}}&lt;!-- Please add new information to relevant articles of the series --&gt;
[[File:ForbiddenCity MaoZedongPortrait (pixinn.net).jpg|200px|thumb|[[Tiananmen]] with a portrait of Mao Zedong]]
[[Constitution of the People's Republic of China|China's constitution]] states that  The People's Republic of China &quot;is a socialist state under the people's democratic dictatorship led by the working class and based on the alliance of workers and peasants,&quot; and that the state organs &quot;apply the principle of democratic centralism.&quot; &lt;ref&gt;Chapter 1, Articles !, 3 [http://www.npc.gov.cn/englishnpc/Constitution/2007-11/15/content_1372962.htm Constitution of the People's Republic of China]&lt;/ref&gt; The PRC is one of the world's few remaining [[socialist state|socialist states openly endorsing communism]] (see [[Ideology of the Communist Party of China]]). The Chinese government has been variously described as communist and socialist, but also as authoritarian and [[Corporatism|corporatist]],&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal|title=China, Corporatism, and the East Asian Model |first1=Jonathan |last1=Unger |first2= Anita |last2=Chan |journal= The Australian Journal of Chinese Affairs |issue= 33 |date=January 1995 |pages= 29â€“53|doi=10.2307/2950087}}&lt;/ref&gt; with heavy restrictions in many areas, most notably against [[Internet censorship in the People's Republic of China|free access to the Internet]], [[freedom of the press]], [[freedom of assembly]], [[reproductive rights|the right to have children]], [[NGO|free formation of social organizations]] and [[freedom of religion]].&lt;ref name=&quot;freedomhouse&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://freedomhouse.org/template.cfm?page=22&amp;year=2011&amp;country=8016|title=Freedom in the World 2011: China|publisher=Freedom House|year=2011|accessdate=19 June 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; Its current political, ideological and economic system has been termed by its leaders as the &quot;[[people's democratic dictatorship]]&quot;, &quot;[[socialism with Chinese characteristics]]&quot; (which is [[Marxism]] adapted to Chinese circumstances) and the &quot;[[socialist market economy]]&quot; respectively.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=Xi reiterates adherence to socialism with Chinese characteristics|url=http://news.xinhuanet.com/english/china/2013-01/05/c_132082389.htm|newspaper=Xinhua|date=5 January 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Communist Party===
[[Constitution of the People's Republic of China|China's constitution]] declares that the country is ruled &quot;under the leadership&quot; of the [[Communist Party of China]] (CPC).&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://english.people.com.cn/constitution/constitution.html
| title=Constitution of the People's Republic of China
|work=People's Daily  |accessdate=14 July 2009 }}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[elections in the People's Republic of China|electoral system]] is pyramidal. Local People's Congresses are [[direct election|directly elected]], and higher levels of People's Congresses up to the [[National People's Congress]] (NPC) are [[indirect election|indirectly elected]] by the People's Congress of the level immediately below.&lt;ref name=&quot;a97&quot;&gt;Article 97 of the [[Constitution of the People's Republic of China]]&lt;/ref&gt; The political system is decentralized, and provincial and sub-provincial leaders have a significant amount of autonomy.&lt;ref name=&quot;cfr&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.cfr.org/publication/14482/communist_party_of_china.html |title=CFR.org |publisher=CFR.org |accessdate=27 April 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[List of political parties in the People's Republic of China|Other political parties]], referred to as democratic parties, have representatives in the National People's Congress and the [[Chinese People's Political Consultative Conference]] (CPPCC).&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Democratic Parties|url=http://english.people.com.cn/data/China_in_brief/Political_Parties/Democratic%20Parties.html|work=People's Daily|accessdate=8 December 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;
[[File:HammerSickle Tiananmen.jpg|thumb|left|200px|Monument in [[Tiananmen Square]] marking the 90th anniversary of the CPC]]

Compared to its closed-door policies until the mid-1970s, the administrative climate is less restrictive than before. China supports the Leninist principle of &quot;[[democratic centralism]]&quot;,&lt;ref&gt;''[[Constitution of the People's Republic of China]]''. (1982)&lt;/ref&gt; but critics describe the elected National People's Congress as a &quot;[[rubber stamp (politics)|rubber stamp]]&quot; body.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/shared/spl/hi/in_depth/china_politics/government/html/7.stm |title=BBC, Country Report: China |publisher=BBC News |accessdate=14 July 2009 }}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Government===
{{Main article|Government of China}}
[[File:Great Hall Of The People At Night.JPG|200px|thumb|The [[Great Hall of the People]] in Beijing, where the [[National People's Congress]] convenes]]
The [[President of the People's Republic of China|President of China]] is the titular [[head of state]], serving as the ceremonial [[figurehead]] under [[National People's Congress]]. The [[Premier of the People's Republic of China|Premier of China]] is the [[head of government]], presiding over the [[State Council of the People's Republic of China|State Council]] composed of four vice premiers and the heads of ministries and commissions. The incumbent president is [[Xi Jinping]], who is also the [[General Secretary of the Communist Party of China]] and the [[Chairman of the Central Military Commission]], making him China's [[paramount leader]].&lt;ref name=&quot;XiJinpingLiKeqiang&quot; /&gt; The incumbent premier is [[Li Keqiang]], who is also a senior member of the [[CPC Politburo Standing Committee]], China's ''de facto'' top decision-making body.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|first = Susan| last = Shirk|title=China's Next Leaders: A Guide to What's at Stake|url=http://www.chinafile.com/chinas-next-leaders-guide-whats-stake|accessdate=31 May 2015|newspaper=China File|date=13 November 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;

There have been some moves toward political liberalization, in that open contested elections are now held at the village and town levels.&lt;ref name=&quot;poll&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.chinadaily.com.cn/en/doc/2003-12/08/content_288018.htm
| title=Beijingers Get Greater Poll Choices
| accessdate=18 February 2007 |work=China Daily |year=2003}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_p&quot;&gt;Lohmar, Bryan; and Somwaru, Agapi; [http://web.archive.org/web/20120201000000/http://www.ers.usda.gov/publications/aib775/aib775n.pdf ''Does China's Land-Tenure System Discourage Structural Adjustment?'']. 1 May 2006. USDA Economic Research Service. Retrieved 3 May 2006.&lt;/ref&gt; However, the Party retains effective control over government appointments: in the absence of meaningful opposition, the CPC wins by default most of the time. Political concerns in China include the growing gap between rich and poor and government corruption.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_q&quot;&gt;[http://www.highbeam.com/doc/1P1-52919430.html &quot;China sounds alarm over fast-growing gap between rich and poor&quot;]. Associated Press via Highbeam (subscription required to see full article). 11 May 2002. Retrieved 1 February 2013.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Hasmath, R. (2012) &quot;[http://www.washingtontimes.com/news/2012/nov/9/red-chinas-iron-grip-on-power/ Red China's Iron Grip on Power: Communist Party Continues Repression]&quot;, The Washington Times, 12 November, p. B4.&lt;/ref&gt; Nonetheless, the level of public support for the government and its management of the nation is high, with 80â€“95% of Chinese citizens expressing satisfaction with the central government, according to a 2011 survey.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=A Point Of View: Is China more legitimate than the West?|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/magazine-20178655|newspaper=BBC News|date=2 November 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Administrative divisions===
{{Main article|Administrative divisions of China|Districts of Hong Kong|Municipalities of Macau}}
The People's Republic of China has administrative control over 22 [[Provinces of China|provinces]] and considers [[Taiwan]] to be [[Taiwan Province, People's Republic of China|its 23rd province]], although [[Taiwan Province, People's Republic of China|Taiwan]] is currently and independently governed by the Republic of China, which disputes the PRC's claim.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_ap&quot;&gt;Gwillim Law (2 April 2005). [http://www.statoids.com/ucn.html Provinces of China]. Retrieved 15 April 2006.&lt;/ref&gt; China also has five subdivisions officially termed [[Autonomous regions of China|autonomous regions]], each with a designated minority group; four [[Direct-controlled municipality of China|municipalities]]; and two [[Special Administrative Region]]s (SARs), which enjoy a degree of political autonomy. These 22 provinces, five autonomous regions, and four municipalities can be collectively referred to as &quot;[[mainland China]]&quot;, a term which usually excludes the SARs of [[Hong Kong]] and [[Macau]]. None of these divisions are recognized by the ROC government, which claims the entirety of the PRC's territory.

{{PRC provinces small imagemap/province list}}
{{PRC provinces big imagemap alt}}

===Foreign relations===
{{Main article|Foreign relations of China}}
[[File:BRICS heads of state and government hold hands ahead of the 2014 G-20 summit in Brisbane, Australia.jpeg|200px|thumb|Chinese President [[Xi Jinping]] holds hands with fellow [[BRICS]] leaders at the [[2014 G20 Brisbane summit]] in Australia]]
The PRC has diplomatic relations with 173 countries and maintains [[List of diplomatic missions of the People's Republic of China|embassies in 162]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Background Note: China|url=http://www.state.gov/r/pa/ei/bgn/18902.htm|work=Bureau of Public Affairs|publisher=US Department of State|accessdate=10 March 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Its [[legitimacy (political)|legitimacy]] is disputed by the Republic of China and a few other countries; it is thus the largest and most populous [[List of states with limited recognition|state with limited recognition]]. In 1971, the PRC replaced the Republic of China as the sole representative of China in the United Nations and as one of the five permanent members of the [[United Nations Security Council]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_r&quot;&gt;Chang, Eddy (22 August 2004). [http://www.taipeitimes.com/News/editorials/archives/2004/08/22/2003199768 ''Perseverance will pay off at the UN''], ''The Taipei Times''.&lt;/ref&gt; China was also a former member and leader of the [[Non-Aligned Movement]], and still considers itself an advocate for [[developing countries]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_2009&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://english.peopledaily.com.cn/90001/90776/90883/6847341.html|title=China says communication with other developing countries at Copenhagen summit transparent|date=21 December 2009|accessdate=20 August 2010|work=People's Daily}}&lt;/ref&gt; Along with Brazil, Russia, India and South Africa, China is a member of the [[BRICS]] group of emerging major economies and hosted the group's [[2011 BRICS summit|third official summit]] at [[Sanya]], [[Hainan]] in April 2011.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-pacific-13076229 &quot;BRICS summit ends in China&quot;]. BBC. 14 April 2011. Retrieved 24 October 2011.&lt;/ref&gt;

Under its interpretation of the [[One-China policy]], Beijing has made it a precondition to establishing diplomatic relations that the other country acknowledges its claim to Taiwan and severs official ties with the government of the Republic of China. Chinese officials have protested on numerous occasions when foreign countries have made diplomatic overtures to Taiwan,&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.mysinchew.com/node/33834
| title=Taiwan's Ma to stopover in US: report
| work=mysinchew.com |date=12 January 2010 }}&lt;/ref&gt; especially in the matter of armament sales.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.timesonline.co.uk/tol/news/world/asia/article7010435.ece |work=The Times |location=London |date=1 February 2010
| title=China says US arms sales to Taiwan could threaten wider relations
| author=Macartney, Jane }}&lt;/ref&gt;

Much of current Chinese foreign policy is reportedly based on Premier [[Zhou Enlai]]'s [[Five Principles of Peaceful Coexistence]], and is also driven by the concept of &quot;harmony without uniformity&quot;, which encourages diplomatic relations between states despite ideological differences.&lt;ref name=&quot;Keith&quot;&gt;{{Cite book|last=Keith|first=Ronald C.|title=China from the inside out â€“ fitting the People's republic into the world|publisher=PlutoPress |pages=135â€“136}}&lt;/ref&gt; This policy may have led China to support states that are [[rogue state|regarded as dangerous]] or repressive by Western nations, such as [[Zimbabwe]], North Korea and [[Iran]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=An Authoritarian Axis Rising?|url=http://thediplomat.com/2012/06/an-authoritarian-axis-rising/|newspaper=The Diplomat|date=29 June 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; China has a close economic and military relationship with Russia,&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.dw.de/china-russia-launch-largest-ever-joint-military-exercise/a-16931106|title=China, Russia launch largest ever joint military exercise|work=Deutsche Welle|date=5 July 2013|accessdate=5 July 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; and the two states often vote in unison in the UN Security Council.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-18327632|title=Energy to dominate Russia President Putin's China visit|publisher=BBC|date=5 June 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.nytimes.com/2012/07/20/world/middleeast/russia-and-china-veto-un-sanctions-against-syria.html|title=Friction at the U.N. as Russia and China Veto Another Resolution on Syria Sanctions|work=[[New York Times]]|date=19 July 2012|accessdate=15 November 2012|first=Rick|last=Gladstone}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-21911842|title=Xi Jinping: Russia-China ties 'guarantee world peace'|publisher=BBC|date=23 March 2013|accessdate=23 March 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;
[[File:Clinton and Biden meet Xi Jinping.jpg|thumb|right|200px|Chinese President [[Xi Jinping]] with U.S. Secretary of State [[Hillary Clinton]] and Vice President [[Joe Biden]], 14 May 2013]]

====Trade relations====
In recent decades, China has played an increasing role in calling for [[free trade area]]s and security pacts amongst its Asia-Pacific neighbours. In 2004, it proposed an entirely new [[East Asia Summit]] (EAS) framework as a forum for regional security issues.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_s&quot;&gt;Dillon, Dana; and Tkacik, John, Jr.; [https://web.archive.org/web/20060210135228/http://www.policyreview.org/134/dillon.html ''China's Quest for Asia'']. ''Policy Review''. December 2005 and January 2006. Issue No. 134. Retrieved 22 April 2006.&lt;/ref&gt; The EAS, which includes [[ASEAN Plus Three]], India, Australia and New Zealand, held its inaugural summit in 2005. China is also a founding member of the Shanghai Cooperation Organization (SCO), along with Russia and the Central Asian republics. China became a member of the World Trade Organization (WTO) on 11 December 2001.

In 2000, the [[United States Congress]] approved &quot;permanent normal trade relations&quot; (PNTR) with China, allowing Chinese exports in at the same low tariffs as goods from most other countries.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=Clinton signs China trade bill|url=http://transcripts.cnn.com/2000/ALLPOLITICS/stories/10/10/clinton.pntr/|publisher=CNN|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20090505165947/http://transcripts.cnn.com/2000/ALLPOLITICS/stories/10/10/clinton.pntr/|archivedate=5 May 2009|date=10 October 2000}}&lt;/ref&gt; China has a significant [[trade surplus]] with the United States, its most important export market.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_w&quot;&gt;&quot;[http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-11544677 US trade gap widens on increased Chinese imports]&quot;. BBC News. 14 October 2010.&lt;/ref&gt; In the early 2010s, US politicians argued that the [[Chinese yuan]] was significantly undervalued, giving China an unfair trade advantage.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_x&quot;&gt;&quot;[http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/business/8617189.stm Chinese President Hu Jintao resists Obama calls on yuan]&quot;. BBC News. 13 April 2010.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;CurrencyManipulator&quot;&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.reuters.com/article/2012/09/24/us-usa-campaign-romney-china-idUSBRE88N12M20120924|title=Obama should call China a currency manipulator: Romney aide|publisher=Reuters|date=24 September 2012|accessdate=6 October 2012|first=Doug|last=Palmer}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-20518490|title=US says China not a currency manipulator|publisher=BBC|date=27 November 2012|accessdate=28 November 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; In recent decades, China has followed a policy of [[Involvement of the People's Republic of China in Africa|engaging with African nations]] for trade and bilateral co-operation;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_ae&quot;&gt;McLaughlin, Abraham; [http://www.csmonitor.com/2005/0330/p01s01-woaf.html &quot;A rising China counters US clout in Africa&quot;]. ''[[Christian Science Monitor]]''. 30 March 2005.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_af&quot;&gt;Lyman, Princeton N.; [http://www.cfr.org/publication/8436/ &quot;China's Rising Role in Africa&quot;]. 21 July 2005. Council of Foreign Relations. Retrieved 26 June 2007.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_ag&quot;&gt;Politzer, Malia. [http://www.migrationinformation.org/Feature/display.cfm?id=690 &quot;China and Africa: Stronger Economic Ties Mean More Migration&quot;]. Migration Information Source. August 2008. Retrieved 26 February 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; in 2012, Sino-African trade totalled over US$160 billion.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.chinadaily.com.cn/business/2012-12/28/content_16063967.htm|title=China-Africa trade likely to hit record high|work=China Daily|date=28 December 2012|accessdate=29 January 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; China has furthermore strengthened its ties with major South American economies, becoming the largest trading partner of Brazil and building strategic links with [[Argentina]].&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.forbes.com/sites/ricardogeromel/2011/08/24/is-brazil-a-derivative-of-china/ &quot;Is Brazil a derivative of China?&quot;]. [[Forbes]].com. 24 August 2011. Retrieved 24 September 2011.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[http://news.xinhuanet.com/english2010/china/2011-09/09/c_131129475.htm &quot;China, Argentina agree to further strategic ties&quot;]. [[Xinhua]].com. 9 September 2011. Retrieved 24 September 2011.&lt;/ref&gt;

====Territorial disputes====
[[File:China administrative.png|thumb|200px|Map depicting territorial disputes between the PRC and neighbouring states. For a larger map, [[Template:PRC provinces big imagemap|see here]]]]
{{main article|Foreign relations of China#International territorial disputes}}
{{See also|List of wars involving the People's Republic of China|One-China policy}}

Ever since its establishment after the second [[Chinese Civil War]], the PRC has been claiming [[Free area of the Republic of China|the territories]] governed by the [[Republic of China]] (ROC), a separate political entity today commonly known as Taiwan, as a part of its territory, which includes the [[island of Taiwan]] as [[Taiwan Province, People's Republic of China|Taiwan Province]], [[Kinmen]] and [[Matsu Islands|Matsu]] as a part of [[Fujian Province]] and islands the ROC controls in the [[South China Sea]] as a part of [[Hainan Province]] and [[Guangdong Province]]. These claims are controversial because of the complicated [[Cross-Strait relations]], and has been [[One-China policy|one of the most important principles]] in Chinese diplomacy.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://history.cultural-china.com/en/34History7320.html|title=Chinese Civil War|publisher=Cultural-China.com|quote=To this day, since no armistice or peace treaty has ever been signed, there is controversy as to whether the Civil War has legally ended.|accessdate=16 June 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

In addition to Taiwan, China is also involved in other international territorial disputes. Since the 1990s, China has been involved in negotiations to resolve its disputed land borders, including a [[Sino-Indian border dispute|disputed border with India]] and an undefined border with [[Bhutan]]. China is additionally involved in multilateral disputes over the ownership of several small islands in the East and South China Seas, such as the [[Senkaku Islands dispute|Senkaku Islands]] and the [[Scarborough Shoal standoff|Scarborough Shoal]].&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-18045383 &quot;China denies preparing war over South China Sea shoal&quot;]. BBC. 12 May 2012.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=Q&amp;A: China-Japan islands row|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-pacific-11341139|publisher=BBC News |date=27 November 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; On 21 May 2014 President Xi, speaking at a conference in Shanghai, pledged to settle China's territorial disputes peacefully. &quot;China stays committed to seeking peaceful settlement of disputes with other countries over territorial sovereignty and maritime rights and interests&quot;, he said.&lt;ref name=&quot;TerritorialDisputes&quot;&gt;{{cite news|title=Asian nations should avoid military ties with third party powers, says China's Xi|url=http://www.chinanationalnews.com/index.php/sid/222207019/scat/9366300fc9319e9b/ht/Asian-nations-should-avoid-military-ties-with-third-party-powers-says-Chinas-Xi|accessdate=21 May 2014|publisher=''China National News''}}&lt;/ref&gt;

====Emerging superpower status====
China is regularly hailed as a [[Potential superpowers|potential new superpower]], with certain commentators citing its rapid economic progress, growing military might, very large population, and increasing international influence as signs that it will play a [[Chinese Century|prominent global role]] in the 21st century.&lt;ref name=&quot;ChinaFuture&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.guardian.co.uk/world/2012/jun/18/china-birth-of-superpower|title=China: witnessing the birth of a superpower|work=The Guardian|date=18 June 2012|accessdate=6 March 2013|location=London|first=Jonathan|last=Watts}}&lt;/ref&gt; Others, however, warn that [[economic bubbles]] and demographic imbalances could slow or even halt China's growth as the century progresses.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web |url=http://www.worldtribune.com/worldtribune/WTARC/2007/s6_29.asp|title=China's utterly distorted economy is a train wreck waiting to happen|work=World Tribune|last=Sanders|first=Sol|date=29 June 2007|accessdate=27 March 2009
}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/138219/ruchir-sharma/broken-brics|title=Broken BRICs: Why the Rest Stopped Rising|work=[[Foreign Affairs]]|date=November 2012|accessdate=19 December 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;
Some authors also question the definition of &quot;superpower&quot;, arguing that China's large economy alone would not qualify it as a superpower, and noting that it lacks the military and cultural influence of the United States.&lt;ref&gt;Grinin, Leonid. [http://www.sociostudies.org/journal/articles/140670/ &quot;Chinese Joker in the World Pack&quot;]. ''Journal of Globalization Studies''. Volume 2, Number 2. November 2011. Retrieved 1 November 2012.&lt;/ref&gt;

===Sociopolitical issues, human rights, and reform===
{{see also|Human rights in China|Hukou system|Social welfare in China|Elections in the People's Republic of China|Censorship in China|Feminism in China}}
[[File:2010-07-25 Guangzhou mass assembly.jpg|thumb|200px|Protests in support of [[Cantonese]] media localization in Guangzhou, 2010]]
The [[Chinese democracy movement]], social activists, and some members of the Communist Party of China have all identified the need for social and political reform. While economic and social controls have been significantly relaxed in China since the 1970s, [[political freedom]] is still tightly restricted. The [[Constitution of the People's Republic of China]] states that the &quot;fundamental rights&quot; of citizens include [[freedom of speech]], [[freedom of the press]], the [[right to a fair trial]], [[freedom of religion]], [[universal suffrage]], and [[property|property rights]]. However, in practice, these provisions do not afford significant protection against criminal prosecution by the state.&lt;ref name=&quot;books.google&quot;&gt;{{cite book|first=Guy |last=Sorman |year= 2008 |title= Empire of Lies: The Truth About China in the Twenty-First Century |pages=46, 152 |url= https://books.google.com/books?id=aRaLevXMZf4C&amp;pg=PA46#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;hrw&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.hrw.org/en/world-report-2009/china |title=World Report 2009: China|publisher=Human Rights Watch |accessdate=14 July 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; Although some criticisms of government policies and the ruling [[Chinese Communist Party|Communist Party]] are tolerated, censorship of political speech and information, most notably on the Internet,&lt;ref&gt;[http://apnews.myway.com//article/20121228/DA3EQG1G1.html &quot;China Requires Internet Users to Register Names&quot;]. AP via My Way News. 28 December 2012. Retrieved 29 December 2012.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;AnonymousNoMore&quot;&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.nytimes.com/2012/12/29/world/asia/china-toughens-restrictions-on-internet-use.html?_r=0|title=China Toughens Its Restrictions on Use of the Internet|work=New York Times|date=28 December 2012|first=Keith|last=Bradsher}}&lt;/ref&gt; are routinely used to prevent collective action.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal |author1=King, Gary |author2=Pan, Jennifer |author3=Roberts, Margaret E.|url=http://gking.harvard.edu/files/gking/files/censored.pdf|title=How Censorship in China Allows Government Criticism but Silences Collective Expression|journal=American Political Science Review|date=May 2013|doi=10.1017/S0003055413000014|accessdate=6 March 2015|quote=Our central theoretical finding is that, contrary to much research and commentary, the purpose of the censorship program is not to suppress criticism of the state or the Communist Party. |volume=107 |pages=326â€“343}}&lt;/ref&gt; In 2005, [[Reporters Without Borders]] ranked China 159th out of 167 states in its Annual World Press Freedom Index, indicating a very low level of press freedom.&lt;ref name=&quot;rsf.org-554&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.rsf.org/rubrique.php3?id_rubrique=554|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20080419011906/http://www.rsf.org/rubrique.php3?id_rubrique=554|archivedate=19 April 2008 |title=Annual Worldwide Press Freedom Index â€“ 2005 |publisher=Reporters Without Borders |date=30 April 2009 |accessdate=14 July 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; In 2014, China ranked 175th out of 180 countries.&lt;ref&gt;[http://rsf.org/index2014/en-index2014.php World Press Freedom Index 2014], Reporters Without Borders, Retrieved 10 March 2015&lt;/ref&gt;

Rural migrants to China's cities often find themselves treated as [[second-class citizen]]s by the ''hukou'' [[hukou system|household registration]] system, which controls access to [[welfare state|state benefits]].&lt;ref name=&quot;ruralmillions&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|last=Wingfield |first=Rupert |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/asia-pacific/4782194.stm |title=China's rural millions left behind |publisher=BBC |date=7 March 2006 |accessdate=14 July 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;hukou&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|last=Luard |first=Tim |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/asia-pacific/4424944.stm |title=China rethinks peasant apartheid |publisher=BBC |date=10 November 2005 |accessdate=14 July 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; Property rights are often poorly protected,&lt;ref name=&quot;ruralmillions&quot; /&gt; and taxation disproportionately affects poorer citizens.&lt;ref name=&quot;hukou&quot; /&gt; However, a number of rural taxes have been reduced or abolished since the early 2000s, and additional social services provided to rural dwellers.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ni2005&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://articles.latimes.com/2005/dec/30/world/fg-agtax30 |title=China to Abolish Contentious Agricultural Levy|work=Los Angeles Times  |date=30 December 2005 |accessdate=27 April 2010 | first=Ching-Ching | last=Ni}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_2006&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/world/asia-pacific/6174847.stm |title= China ends school fees for 150m|publisher=BBC |date=13 December 2006 |accessdate=27 April 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;

A number of foreign governments, foreign press agencies and [[NGO]]s also routinely criticize [[Human rights in the People's Republic of China|China's human rights record]], alleging widespread [[civil rights]] violations such as detention without trial, [[forced abortions]],&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://apnews.myway.com//article/20140109/DAB75AAG2.html|date=9 January 2014|author=Didi Tang|title=Forced abortion highlights abuses in China policy |publisher=Associated Press}}&lt;/ref&gt; forced confessions, [[torture]], restrictions of fundamental rights,&lt;ref name=&quot;freedomhouse&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;XinBan2012&quot; /&gt; and [[Capital punishment in the People's Republic of China|excessive use of the death penalty]].&lt;ref name=&quot;wp&quot;&gt;{{cite news| url=http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-dyn/content/article/2008/12/23/AR2008122302795.html | work=[[The Washington Post]] | title=China's Capital Cases Still Secret, Arbitrary | first1=Maureen | last1=Fan | first2=Ariana Eunjung | last2=Cha | date=24 December 2008 | accessdate=16 August 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=https://ph.news.yahoo.com/amnesty-sees-hope-china-death-penalty-011032864.html|title=Amnesty sees hope in China on death penalty|work= Yahoo news|date=27 March 2012|accessdate=31 May 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; The government has suppressed popular protests and demonstrations that it considers a potential threat to &quot;social stability&quot;, as was the case with the [[Tiananmen Square protests of 1989]].

[[Falun Gong]] was first taught publicly in 1992. In 1999, when there were 70 million practitioners,&lt;ref name=Faison&gt;Seth Faison, [http://partners.nytimes.com/library/world/asia/042799china-protest.html &quot;In Beijing: A Roar of Silent Protestors&quot;], [[New York Times]], 27 April 1999&lt;/ref&gt; the [[persecution of Falun Gong]] began, resulting in mass arrests, extralegal detention, and reports of torture and deaths in custody.&lt;ref name=Amnesty2013&gt;{{cite book|last1=Amnesty International|title=Changing the soup but not the medicine: Abolishing re-education through labor in China|date=Dec 2013|location=London,UK|url=https://www.amnesty.nl/sites/default/files/public/rtl_briefing_dec_2013_asa_17_042_2013_final1.pdf}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite book|first=Mickey |last=Spiegel |url=http://hrw.org/reports/2002/china/ |title=Dangerous Meditation: China's Campaign Against Falungong |publisher=Human Rights Watch |year=2002 |isbn=1-56432-269-6|ref=harv}}&lt;/ref&gt; The Chinese state is regularly accused of large-scale repression and human rights abuses in [[Tibet]] and [[Xinjiang]], including violent police crackdowns and [[religious suppression]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-23081653|title=China 'moves two million Tibetans'|publisher=BBC|date=27 June 2013|accessdate=27 June 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-23112177|title=Fresh unrest hits China's Xinjiang|publisher=BBC|date=29 June 2013|accessdate=29 June 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The Chinese government has responded to foreign criticism by arguing that the right to subsistence and economic development is a prerequisite to other types of human rights, and that the notion of human rights should take into account a country's present level of [[economic development]].&lt;ref name=&quot;yqlgro&quot;&gt;[http://www.gov.cn/english/official/2005-07/28/content_18115.htm &quot;China's Progress in Human Rights in 2004&quot;]. Gov.cn. July 2005. Retrieved 31 May 2015.&lt;/ref&gt; It emphasizes the rise in the Chinese [[standard of living]], [[literacy rate]] and average [[life expectancy]] since the 1970s, as well as improvements in workplace safety and efforts to combat natural disasters such as the perennial [[Yangtze River]] floods.&lt;ref name=&quot;yqlgro&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.usatoday.com/news/world/2008-01-30-chinasafety_N.htm &quot;China seeks to improve workplace safety&quot;]. ''[[USA Today]]''. 30 January 2008. Retrieved 15 May 2012.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_ao&quot;&gt;[http://www.china-embassy.org/eng/zt/first%20beginning/t56058.htm &quot;China's reform and opening-up promotes human rights, says premier&quot;]. Embassy of the People's Republic of China in the United States. 11 December 2003. Retrieved 28 April 2006.&lt;/ref&gt; Furthermore, some Chinese politicians have spoken out in support of democratization, although [[Chinese skepticism of democracy|others remain more conservative]].&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-dyn/content/article/2010/10/13/AR2010101306347.html &quot;Chinese Premier Wen Jiabao talks reform, but most countrymen never get to hear what he says&quot;]. ''[[Washington Post]]''. 13 October 2010. Retrieved 6 July 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; Some major reform efforts have been conducted; for an instance in November 2013, the government announced plans to relax the one-child policy and abolish the much-criticized [[re-education through labour]] program,&lt;ref name=SlateChina2013&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.slate.com/blogs/moneybox/2013/11/15/china_reforms_one_child_policy_little_siblings_coming.html|title=China ends one child policy|work=Slate|date=15 November 2013|accessdate=16 November 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; though human rights groups note that reforms to the latter have been largely cosmetic.&lt;ref name=Amnesty2013/&gt; During the 2000s and early 2010s, the Chinese government was increasingly tolerant of NGOs that offer practical, efficient solutions to social problems, but such &quot;third sector&quot; activity remained heavily regulated.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.dandc.eu/articles/220672/index.en.shtml |title=Service providers wanted|work=Development and Cooperation|date=2 August 2012|accessdate=11 September 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;

==Military==
{{Main article|People's Liberation Army}}
[[File:J-10a zhas.png|thumb|200px|A [[People's Liberation Army Air Force|PLAAF]] [[Chengdu J-10]] fighter aircraft]]
[[File:Luyang II (Type 052C) Class Destroyer.JPG|thumb|200px|The ''Lanzhou'' (DDG170), a [[Type 052C destroyer]] of the PLAN]]
With 2.3&amp;nbsp;million active troops, the People's Liberation Army (PLA) is the largest standing military force in the world, commanded by the [[Central Military Commission (People's Republic of China)|Central Military Commission]] (CMC).&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_abcdep&quot;&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-20318047|title=The new generals in charge of China's guns|publisher=BBC|date=14 November 2012|accessdate=10 December 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; The PLA consists of the [[People's Liberation Army Ground Force|Ground Force]] (PLAGF), the [[People's Liberation Army Navy|Navy]] (PLAN), the [[People's Liberation Army Air Force|Air Force]] (PLAAF), and the [[People's Liberation Army Rocket Force]] (PLARF). According to the Chinese government, China's military budget for 2014 totalled US$132 billion, constituting the [[List of countries by military expenditures|world's second-largest military budget]].&lt;ref name=SIPRI2014/&gt; However, many authorities â€“ including [[SIPRI]] and the U.S. [[Office of the Secretary of Defense]] â€“ argue that China does not report its real level of military spending, which is allegedly much higher than the official budget.&lt;ref name=SIPRI2014&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.sipri.org/media/newsletter/essay/perlo-freeman-mar-2013|title=Mar. 2014: Deciphering China's latest defence budget figures|publisher=[[SIPRI]]|date=March 2014|accessdate=9 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_abcdeq&quot;&gt;[http://www.defenselink.mil/pubs/pdfs/China_Military_Power_Report_2009.pdf Annual Report To Congress â€“ Military Power of the People's Republic of China 2009 (PDF)]. Defenselink.mil. Retrieved 27 November 2011.&lt;/ref&gt;

As a recognized [[nuclear weapons]] state, China is considered both a major regional military power and a [[Potential superpowers|potential military superpower]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_abcder&quot;&gt;Nolt, James H. [http://www.atimes.com/china/BA27Ad01.html Analysis: The China-Taiwan military balance]. ''Asia Times''. 1999. Retrieved 15 April 2006.&lt;/ref&gt; According to a 2013 report by the [[US Department of Defense]], China fields between 50 and 75 nuclear [[ICBM]]s, along with a number of [[Short-range ballistic missile|SRBMs]].&lt;ref name=&quot;ChineseNukes&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.defense.gov/pubs/2013_China_Report_FINAL.pdf|title=Military and Security Developments Involving the People's Republic of China 2013|publisher=US Secretary of Defense|format=PDF|year=2013|accessdate=25 June 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; However, compared with the other four [[UN Security Council]] Permanent Members, China has relatively limited [[Military power projection|power projection]] capabilities.&lt;ref name=&quot;Martin&quot;&gt;{{cite web|first=Martin |last=Andrew |url=http://www.asianresearch.org/articles/2680.html |title=THE DRAGON BREATHES FIRE: CHINESE POWER PROJECTION |publisher=AsianResearch.org|date=18 August 2005 |accessdate=26 June 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; To offset this, it has developed numerous power projection assets since the early 2000s â€“ its [[Chinese aircraft carrier Liaoning|first aircraft carrier]] entered service in 2012,&lt;ref name=&quot;J15Carrier&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web | title= China's first aircraft carrier completes sea trial | url= http://news.xinhuanet.com/english2010/video/2011-08/15/c_131050307.htm | publisher= Xinhua News Agency | date= 15 August 2011 | accessdate=15 August 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://online.wsj.com/article/SB10000872396390444358804578017481172611110.html|title=China: Aircraft Carrier Now in Service|date=25 September 2012|accessdate=26 September 2012|work=The Wall Street Journal}}&lt;/ref&gt; and it maintains a substantial fleet of [[submarines]], including several [[nuclear submarine|nuclear-powered]] [[nuclear attack submarine|attack]] and [[ballistic missile submarine|ballistic missile]] submarines.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.guardian.co.uk/world/2009/apr/22/china-submarines-foreign-relations &quot;China unveils fleet of submarines&quot;]. ''[[The Guardian]]''. 22 April 2009. Retrieved 16 October 2011.&lt;/ref&gt; China has furthermore established a [[String of Pearls (China)|network of foreign military relationships along critical sea lanes]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://articles.timesofindia.indiatimes.com/2013-05-30/india/39627850_1_indian-ocean-india-and-japan-japanese-counterpart-shinzo-abe|title=India, Japan join hands to break China's 'string of pearls'|work=Times of India|date=30 May 2013|accessdate=7 July 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

China has made significant progress in modernising its air force in recent decades, purchasing Russian fighter jets such as the [[Sukhoi Su-30]], and also manufacturing its own modern fighters, most notably the [[Chengdu J-10]], [[Chengdu J-20|J-20]] and the [[Shenyang J-11]], [[Shenyang J-15|J-15]], [[J-16]], and [[Shenyang J-31|J-31]].&lt;ref name=&quot;J15Carrier&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.flightglobal.com/news/articles/in-focus-long-march-ahead-for-chinese-naval-airpower-379419/|title=IN FOCUS: Long march ahead for Chinese naval airpower|publisher=Flightglobal.com|date=26 November 2012|accessdate=26 November 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_2009f&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.sinodefence.com/airforce/fighter/j10b.asp |title=J-10 |publisher=SinoDefence.com |date=28 March 2009 |accessdate=27 April 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; China is furthermore engaged in developing an indigenous [[stealth aircraft]] and numerous [[unmanned aerial vehicle|combat drones]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.popsci.com/technology/article/2012-12/inside-chinas-secret-arsenal|title=Inside China's Secret Arsenal|work=[[Popular Science]]|date=20 December 2012|accessdate=20 December 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[http://china-defense.blogspot.com/ &quot;Early Eclipse: F-35 JSF Prospects in the Age of Chinese Stealth.&quot;] China-Defense. Retrieved 23 January 2011.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Defense Update&quot;&gt;[http://www.defense-update.com/products/j/29122010_j-20.html &quot;Chengdu J-20 â€“ China's 5th Generation Fighter.&quot;] Defense-Update.com. Retrieved 23 January 2011.&lt;/ref&gt; [[Air supremacy|Air]] and [[Sea denial]] weaponry advances have increased the regional threat from the perspective of Japan as well as Washington.&lt;ref&gt;Washington Journal. (12 August 2015) &quot;U.S. Military Approach toward China&quot;. Mark Perry, Politico writer, interview by Steve Scanlan, host. C-Span. Retrieved 12 August 2015. [http://www.c-span.org/video/?327492-5/washington-journal-mark-perry-us-military-approach-toward-china C-Span website]&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Al Jazeera America Wire Service. (11 May 2015) Japan moves to boost role of military. Retrieved 12 August 2015. [http://america.aljazeera.com/articles/2015/5/11/japan-moves-to-boost-role-of-military.html Al Jazerra America website]&lt;/ref&gt; China has also updated its ground forces, replacing its ageing [[Soviet Union|Soviet]]-derived [[main battle tank|tank]] inventory with numerous variants of the modern [[Type 99 tank]], and upgrading its battlefield [[C4ISTAR|C3I]] and [[C4I]] systems to enhance its [[network-centric warfare]] capabilities.&lt;ref&gt;[http://web.archive.org/web/20131104200224/http://www.sinodefence.com/army/default.asp Ground Forces]. SinoDefence.com. Retrieved 31 May 2015.&lt;/ref&gt; In addition, China has developed or acquired numerous advanced missile systems,&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_abcdes&quot;&gt;[http://web.archive.org/web/20130904130239/http://www.sinodefence.com/army/surfacetoairmissile/hongqi9.asp Surface-to-air Missile System]. SinoDefence.com. 2006. Retrieved 31 May 2015.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_2008e&quot;&gt;{{cite book| chapter=HQ-19 (S-400) (China)|title=Jane's Weapons: Strategic |publisher=IHS |date=23 December 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt; including [[2007 Chinese anti-satellite missile test|anti-satellite missiles]],&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.channelnewsasia.com/stories/afp_asiapacific/view/253580/1/.html &quot;China plays down fears after satellite shot down&quot;]. [[Agence France-Presse]] via [[MediaCorp Channel NewsAsia|ChannelNewsAsia]]. 20 January 2007. Retrieved 11 July 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; [[cruise missile]]s&lt;ref&gt;[http://asw.newpacificinstitute.org/?p=11412 &quot;Chinese Navy Tests Land Attack Cruise Missiles: Implications for Asia-Pacific&quot;]. New Pacific Institute. 25 July 2012. Retrieved 1 October 2012.&lt;/ref&gt; and submarine-launched nuclear ICBMs.&lt;ref name=&quot;WashTiNu&quot;&gt;[http://www.washingtontimes.com/news/2011/aug/25/beijing-to-expand-its-nuclear-stockpile/?page=all &quot;China expanding its nuclear stockpile&quot;]. ''[[The Washington Times]]''. 25 August 2011. Retrieved 16 October 2011.&lt;/ref&gt; According to the [[Stockholm International Peace Research Institute]]'s data, China became the world's third largest exporter of major arms in 2010â€“14, an increase of 143 per cent from the period 2005â€“09.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=The United States leads upward trend in arms exports, Asian and Gulf states arms imports up, says SIPRI|url=http://www.sipri.org/media/pressreleases/2015/at-march-2015|website=www.sipri.org|publisher=Stockholm International Peace Research Institute (SIPRI)|accessdate=18 March 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;
{{clear}}

==Economy==
{{Main article|Economy of China|Agriculture in China|List of Chinese administrative divisions by GDP}}
[[File:Graph of Major Developing Economies by Real GDP per capita at PPP 1990-2013.png|thumb|200px|China and other major developing economies by [[List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita|GDP per capita at purchasing-power parity]], 1990â€“2013. The rapid economic growth of China (red) is readily apparent.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=World Bank World Development Indicators|url=http://data.worldbank.org/data-catalog/world-development-indicators|publisher=World Bank|accessdate=8 December 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt;]]
[[File:Shanghaistockexchange.jpg|thumb|200px|The [[Shanghai Stock Exchange]] building in [[Shanghai]]'s [[Lujiazui]] financial district. Shanghai has the [[List of cities by GDP|25th-largest city GDP]] in the world, totalling US$304 billion in 2011&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.chinadaily.com.cn/china/2012-01/20/content_14484614.htm &quot;Shanghai's GDP grows 8.2% in 2011&quot;]. ''China Daily''. 20 January 2012. Retrieved 15 April 2012.&lt;/ref&gt;]]
{{As of|2014}}, China has [[List of countries by GDP (nominal)|the world's second-largest economy]] in terms of nominal GDP, totalling approximately US$10.380 trillion according to the [[International Monetary Fund]].&lt;ref name=&quot;China GDP&quot;/&gt; If [[purchasing power parity]] (PPP) is taken into account, China's economy is the largest in the world, with a 2014 PPP GDP of US$17.617 trillion.&lt;ref name=&quot;China GDP&quot;/&gt; In 2013, its PPP GDP per capita was US$12,880, while its nominal GDP per capita was US$7,589. Both cases put China [[List of countries by GDP (nominal) per capita|behind around eighty countries]] (out of 183 countries on the IMF list) in global GDP per capita rankings.&lt;ref name=imf2&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.imf.org/external/pubs/ft/weo/2014/02/weodata/weorept.aspx?pr.x=55&amp;pr.y=9&amp;sy=2014&amp;ey=2014&amp;scsm=1&amp;ssd=1&amp;sort=country&amp;ds=.&amp;br=1&amp;c=924&amp;s=NGDPD%2CNGDPDPC%2CPPPGDP%2CPPPPC&amp;grp=0&amp;a=|title=Estimates for 2014 nominal GDP|publisher=International Monetary Fund|date=2014|accessdate=10 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Economic history and growth===
{{main article|Economic history of China (1949â€“present)}}
From its founding in 1949 until late 1978, the People's Republic of China was a Soviet-style centrally [[planned economy]]. Following Mao's death in 1976 and the consequent end of the [[Cultural Revolution]], [[Deng Xiaoping]] and the new Chinese leadership began to [[Economic reform in the People's Republic of China|reform the economy]] and move towards a more market-oriented [[mixed economy]] under one-party rule. [[Collective farming|Agricultural collectivization]] was dismantled and farmlands privatized, while foreign trade became a major new focus, leading to the creation of [[Special Economic Zone]]s (SEZs). Inefficient [[Government-owned corporation|state-owned enterprises]] (SOEs) were restructured and unprofitable ones were closed outright, resulting in massive job losses. Modern-day China is mainly characterized as having a market economy based on private property ownership,&lt;ref name=&quot;english.eastday&quot;&gt;{{cite web |url=http://english.eastday.com/e/ICS/u1a4035916.html |title=China is already a market economyâ€”Long Yongtu, Secretary General of Boao Forum for Asia|publisher=EastDay.com |year=2008 |accessdate=14 July 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; and is one of the leading examples of [[state capitalism]].&lt;ref&gt;[http://blogs.forbes.com/greatspeculations/2010/03/22/communism-is-dead-but-state-capitalism-thrives/ &quot;Communism Is Dead, But State Capitalism Thrives&quot;]. Vahan Janjigian. ''Forbes''. 22 March 2010. Retrieved 11 July 2013.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[http://blogs.forbes.com/gadyepstein/2010/08/31/the-winners-and-losers-in-chinese-capitalism/ &quot;The Winners And Losers In Chinese Capitalism&quot;]. Gady Epstein. ''Forbes''. 31 August 2010. Retrieved 11 July 2013.&lt;/ref&gt; The state still dominates in strategic &quot;pillar&quot; sectors such as energy production and [[heavy industry|heavy industries]], but private enterprise has expanded enormously, with around 30&amp;nbsp;million private businesses recorded in 2008.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_abf&quot;&gt;John Lee. [https://web.archive.org/web/20080726102845/http://www.cis.org.au/issue_analysis/IA95/ia95.html &quot;Putting Democracy in China on Hold&quot;]. The Center for Independent Studies. 26 July 2008. Retrieved 16 July 2013.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Englishpeopledailycomcn2005&quot;&gt;{{cite web|author=English@peopledaily.com.cn |url=http://english.people.com.cn/200507/13/eng20050713_195876.html |title=People.com |work=People |date=13 July 2005 |accessdate=27 April 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_2005a&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.businessweek.com/magazine/content/05_34/b3948478.htm |title=Businessweek.com |work=BusinessWeek |date=22 August 2005 |accessdate=27 April 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_abg&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.oecd.org/dataoecd/16/3/36174313.pdf |title=Microsoft Word â€“ China2bandes.doc |format=PDF |publisher=OECD|accessdate=27 April 2010|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20081010154017/http://www.oecd.org:80/dataoecd/16/3/36174313.pdf|archivedate=2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[File:Shanghai - Nanjing Road.jpeg|200px|thumb|left|[[Nanjing Road (Shanghai)|Nanjing Road]], a major shopping street in Shanghai]]

Since economic liberalization began in 1978, China has been among the world's fastest-growing economies,&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=China's Economic Rise: History, Trends, Challenges, and Implications for the United States |url=https://fas.org/sgp/crs/row/RL33534.pdf|publisher=Congressional Research Service |date=5 September 2013 }}&lt;/ref&gt; relying largely on investment- and export-led growth.&lt;ref name=&quot;chinadaily&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.chinadaily.com.cn/bizchina/2008-11/21/content_7228346.htm|title=China must be cautious in raising consumption|work=China Daily |accessdate=8 February 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; According to the IMF, China's [[annual average GDP growth]] between 2001 and 2010 was 10.5%. Between 2007 and 2011, China's economic growth rate was equivalent to all of the [[G7]] countries' growth combined.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|last=Walker |first=Andrew |url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-13802453 |title=Will China's Economy Stumble? |publisher=BBC |date=16 June 2011 |accessdate=1 November 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; According to the [[3G (countries)|Global Growth Generators]] index announced by [[Citigroup]] in February 2011, China has a very high 3G growth rating.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=Joe Weisenthal|url=http://www.businessinsider.com/willem-buiter-3g-countries-2011-2?slop=1 |title=3G Countries |publisher=Businessinsider.com |date=22 February 2011 |accessdate=1 November 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Its high productivity, low labor costs and relatively good infrastructure have made it a global leader in manufacturing. However, the Chinese economy is highly energy-intensive and inefficient;&lt;ref name=&quot;China Quick Facts&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://web.worldbank.org/WBSITE/EXTERNAL/COUNTRIES/EASTASIAPACIFICEXT/CHINAEXTN/0,,contentMDK:20680895~pagePK:1497618~piPK:217854~theSitePK:318950,00.html|title=China Quick Facts|publisher=[[World Bank]]|accessdate=26 July 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt; China became the world's largest [[World energy resources and consumption|energy consumer]] in 2010,&lt;ref name=&quot;Swartz2010&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://online.wsj.com/article/SB10001424052748703720504575376712353150310.html?mod=djemalertNEWS|title=China Becomes World's Biggest Energy Consumer|date=19 July 2010|work=Wall Street Journal |accessdate=19 July 2010 | first1=Spencer | last1=Swartz | first2=Shai | last2=Oster}}&lt;/ref&gt; relies on coal to supply over 70% of its energy needs, and surpassed the US to become the world's largest oil importer in September 2013.&lt;ref name=&quot;BusInsEnergyGuide&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.businessinsider.com/china-energy-use-2012-8?op=1|title=The Ultimate Guide To China's Voracious Energy Use|work=Business Insider|date=17 August 2012|accessdate=12 December 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=China overtakes US as the biggest importer of oil|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-24475934|accessdate=11 October 2013|publisher=BBC|date=10 October 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; In the early 2010s, China's economic growth rate began to slow amid domestic credit troubles, weakening international demand for Chinese exports and fragility in the global economy.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-19975112|title=China's economy slows but data hints at rebound|publisher=BBC|date=18 October 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://mobile.bloomberg.com/news/2013-06-24/china-loses-control-of-its-frankenstein-economy.html|title=China Loses Control of Its Frankenstein Economy|publisher=[[Bloomberg L.P.]]|date=24 June 2013|accessdate=25 June 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://finance.fortune.cnn.com/2013/07/15/china-economy-slowdown/ |title=The lowdown on China's slowdown: It's not all bad |publisher=CNN Money|date=15 July 2013 |accessdate=16 July 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

In the online realm, China's [[e-commerce]] industry has grown more slowly than the EU and the US, with a significant period of development occurring from around 2009 onwards. According to [[Credit Suisse]], the total value of online transactions in China grew from an insignificant size in 2008 to around RMB 4 trillion (US$660 billion) in 2012. The Chinese online payment market is dominated by major firms such as [[Alipay (payment platform)|Alipay]], [[Tencent#TenPay|Tenpay]] and [[China UnionPay]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=China's Internet Giants Lead in Online Finance|url=http://www.thefinancialist.com/not-just-a-paypal-clone-chinas-internet-giants-chart-their-own-course/|work=The Financialist|publisher=Credit Suisse|accessdate=15 February 2014|author=John Watling|date=14 February 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===China in the global economy===
China is a member of the [[World Trade Organization|WTO]] and is the world's largest trading power, with a total international trade value of US$3.87&amp;nbsp;trillion in 2012.&lt;ref name=&quot;ChinaBiggestTrader&quot; /&gt; [[Foreign exchange reserves of the People's Republic of China|Its foreign exchange reserves]] reached US$2.85&amp;nbsp;trillion by the end of 2010, an increase of 18.7% over the previous year, making its reserves by far the world's largest.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_2009b&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.bloomberg.com/apps/news?pid=20601087&amp;sid=alZgI4B1lt3s|title=China's Foreign-Exchange Reserves Surge, Exceeding $2&amp;nbsp;Trillion|date=15 July 2009|work=[[Bloomberg L.P.]]|accessdate=19 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://smetimes.tradeindia.com/smetimes/news/global-business/2011/Jan/11/china-s-forex-reserves-reach-usd-2.85-trillion624606.html |title=China's forex reserves reach USD 2.85 trillion |publisher=Smetimes.tradeindia.com |accessdate=1 November 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; In 2012, China was the world's largest recipient of inward [[foreign direct investment]] (FDI), attracting $253 billion.&lt;ref name=&quot;FDI&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=FDI in Figures|url=http://www.oecd.org/daf/inv/FDI%20in%20figures.pdf|publisher=OECD|accessdate=28 November 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; In 2014, China's foreign exchange remittances were $US64 billion making it the second largest recipient of remittances in the world.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/1176411|title=Pakistanâ€™s remittances|author=Sakib Sherani|work=dawn.com|accessdate=17 December 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; China also invests abroad, with a total outward FDI of $62.4&amp;nbsp;billion in 2012,&lt;ref name=&quot;FDI&quot;/&gt; and a number of major takeovers of foreign firms by Chinese companies.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=Being eaten by the dragon|url=http://www.economist.com/node/17460954|newspaper=The Economist|date=11 November 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; In 2009, China owned an estimated $1.6&amp;nbsp;trillion of US [[security (finance)|securities]],&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_2009c&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.reuters.com/article/bondsNews/idUSPEK16627420090820|title=China must keep buying US Treasuries for now-paper|agency=Reuters|accessdate=19 August 2009 | date=20 August 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; and was also the largest foreign holder of [[US public debt]], owning over $1.16 trillion in US [[Treasury bond]]s.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_abe&quot;&gt;[http://money.cnn.com/2009/07/29/news/economy/china_america_lender_respect.fortune/index.htm &quot;Washington learns to treat China with care&quot;]. CNNMoney.com. 29 July 2009.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Hornby2009&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.reuters.com/article/newsOne/idUSTRE58M25U20090923|title=Factbox: US-China Interdependence Outweighs Trade Spat|publisher=Reuters|date=23 September 2009|accessdate=25 September 2009 | first=Lucy | last=Hornby}}&lt;/ref&gt; China's undervalued exchange rate has caused friction with other major economies,&lt;ref name=&quot;CurrencyManipulator&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_2008&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.chinadaily.com.cn/china/2008-01/11/content_6387775.htm|title=2007 trade surplus hits new record â€“ $262.2B|date=11 January 2008|work=China Daily |accessdate=19 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ref_2005&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.china-embassy.org/eng/gyzg/t213645.htm|title=China widens yuan, non-dollar trading range to 3%|date=23 September 2005|accessdate=19 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; and it has also been widely criticized for manufacturing large quantities of [[counterfeit]] goods.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.asiabusinesscouncil.org/docs/IntellectualPropertyRights.pdf Intellectual Property Rights]. Asia Business Council. September 2005. Retrieved 13 January 2012.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web | url = http://web.mit.edu/cis/fpi_china.html| title= MIT CIS: Publications: Foreign Policy Index | accessdate =15 May 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; According to consulting firm [[McKinsey &amp; Company|McKinsey]], total outstanding debt in China increased from $7.4 trillion in 2007 to $28.2 trillion in 2014, which reflects 228% of China's GDP, a percentage higher than that of some G20 nations.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web|url = http://www.businessinsider.com/germanys-finance-minister-is-worried-about-chinas-debt-and-shadow-banking-2015-4|title =Germany's finance minister is worried about China's debt and shadow banking |date =16 April 2015 |publisher =Business Insider |last =Scutt |first =David }}&lt;/ref&gt;
